Sexual Intercourse During Hajj/Umrah and the Dispute on Separation
Ruling with inter-madhhab dispute on separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on intercourse at different stages of Hajj/Umrah, compare them with the positions of Malik, Zufar, and al-Shafi'i on separation, and explain why the Hanafi school considers separation recommended but not obligatory.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school does not require spouses to separate after intercourse during Hajj/Umrah, while other schools do, based on companion reports interpreted as caution not obligation.

This passage from the matn and its commentary addresses a sensitive but important issue: the effect of sexual intercourse during Hajj and Umrah at various stages. Beyond the rulings on invalidation and required sacrifices, there is a significant inter-madhhab dispute on whether the couple must physically separate while making up the spoiled acts. The Hanafi school takes a lenient position, viewing separation as recommended but not obligatory, while Malik, Zufar, and al-Shafi'i (in his old opinion) require it at different points. Understanding this dispute clarifies the practical differences between the schools.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi rulings on intercourse at different stages
The matn provides the ruling: after standing at Arafat (wuquf), intercourse does not invalidate Hajj but requires a camel (badanah) and qada (making up the Hajj); after shaving (halq), it requires only a sheep (shah). For Umrah, intercourse before completing four circumambulations invalidates it, requiring the pilgrim to proceed, slaughter, and make up; after four circuits, it does not invalidate but requires a sheep.
These rulings establish the context for the dispute on separation.
2
Identify the dispute on separation (iftiraq)
The commentary then discusses whether the couple must separate during the qada (making up) of the spoiled acts. The Hanafi position is that separation is not obligatory but recommended (احتياط وأولوية). Malik, Zufar, and al-Shafi'i (old opinion) hold that separation is obligatory at different points: Malik at leaving the house, Zufar at assuming ihram, and al-Shafi'i at the location of the transgression.
The dispute centers on the legal interpretation of companion narrations.
3
Examine the proofs of the opposing schools
Malik relies on a narration from Ali (رضي الله عنه) ordering separation from the time of ihram, recorded in al-Muwatta. Al-Shafi'i (old opinion) relies on a narration from Umar (رضي الله عنه) ordering separation when the couple reaches the place of transgression, recorded by Ibn Abi Shaybah.
These narrations are the primary evidence for the obligation of separation.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school interprets these narrations as recommendatory (احتياط وأولوية) rather than obligatory. The reasoning is that the purpose is deterrence and prevention of recurrence, but there is no explicit command or principle of obligation. The school upholds its position by considering the companion reports as guidance, not binding legal commands.
This reflects the Hanafi usul principle of preferring caution without imposing obligation.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In practice, Hanafis do not require separation, while Malikis and Shafi'is (old) do. This affects the couple's actions during the qada: Hanafis permit them to remain together, while others require them to separate at specific points. Notably, al-Shafi'i's new opinion agrees with the Hanafi school, resolving the dispute for the Shafi'i school.
The thamarah al-khilaf shows the practical impact of the differing legal positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(وبعد وقوفه لم يفسد ، ويجب بدنة، وبعد الحلق شاة. وفي عمرته قبل طوافه أربعة أشواط مفسد لها ، فمضى وذبح وقضى ، وبعد أربعة ذبح ولم تفسد) : أي وطؤه في عمرته قبل أن يطوف أربعة أشواط مفسد للعمرة، فيجب المضي فيها، والذبح والقضاء، وبعد أربعة أشواط يجب به الذبح ولا تفسد به العمرة. And after his standing (at Arafat) it does not invalidate, and a camel is obligatory, and after the shaving a sheep. And in his Umrah before his circumambulation of four circuits it invalidates it, so he proceeds, slaughters, and makes up; and after four (circuits) he slaughters and it does not invalidate. Meaning he does not have to separate from her in making up what they spoiled; according to Malik he separates when they leave their house; according to Zufar he separates when they assume ihram; according to al-Shafi'i he separates when they reach the place where he had intercourse with her. (And after his standing it does not invalidate, and a camel is obligatory, and after the shaving a sheep. And in his Umrah before his circumambulation of four circuits it invalidates it, so he proceeds, slaughters, and makes up; and after four (circuits) he slaughters and it does not invalidate): i.e., his intercourse in his Umrah before he circumambulates four circuits invalidates the Umrah, so it is obligatory to proceed in it, slaughter, and make up; and after four circuits it obligates slaughter and the Umrah is not invalidated by it. [1] He said: 'According to Malik... etc.'; the reason for separation is to deter them, and so that they do not recall what they did and fall into it again. It is narrated from Ali that he ordered separation from the time of ihram—recorded by Malik in al-Muwatta, and he (Malik) chose it, and Zufar also held this. And from Umar that he ordered separation when they reach the place where they committed the transgression—recorded by Ibn Abi Shaybah, and al-Shafi'i chose this. All this, according to us, is carried upon caution and preference, not obligation. [2] He said: 'When they leave'—thus mentioned in al-Hidayah and others; what is mentioned in most Maliki books is separation at ihram. [3] He said: 'According to al-Shafi'i'—i.e., in his old opinion; as for his new opinion, he agrees with us. [4] He said: 'And after his standing... etc.'; meaning if he has intercourse after completing the standing at Arafat, which is the greatest pillar, his Hajj is not invalidated, but he must slaughter a camel or cow and make up; and if he has intercourse after shaving before circumambulation, he must slaughter a sheep due to the lightness of the transgression. (5) He said: 'It invalidates the Umrah'; because when he has not circumambulated four circuits, the majority (of the Umrah) has not occurred, contrary to the case when he has circumambulated four, for then the majority has occurred, so the transgression becomes light.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn with extensive commentary discussing the effect of sexual intercourse during Hajj and Umrah at different stages, and an inter-madhhab dispute on whether the couple must separate after such an act. The ruling divides the scenarios: after standing at Arafat, intercourse does not invalidate Hajj but requires a badanah (camel) and qada (making up the Hajj); after shaving (halq) but before tawaf, it requires only a sheep. For Umrah, intercourse before completing four tawaf circuits invalidates the Umrah, requiring the person to proceed, slaughter, and make up the Umrah; after four circuits, it does not invalidate but requires a sheep. The commentary explains that the difference is because the majority of the Umrah is considered complete after four circuits, lessening the violation. Then the commentary discusses the issue of separation (iftiraq) as a deterrent, reporting views from Malik, Zufar, and al-Shafi'i (old opinion) that separation is obligatory at different points (leaving home, ihram, location of intercourse). The Hanafi position is that separation is recommended but not obligatory (احتياط وأولوية, not وجوب). The text also notes that al-Shafi'i's new opinion agrees with the Hanafi school. The reasoning for separation is to prevent recurrence. The passage additionally clarifies that after Arafat, the Hajj is not invalidated because the greatest pillar is completed, while after shaving it is lighter.

Key Terms
بدنة (badanah): A camel or cow that is sacrificed as penalty for a transgression during Hajj.
وقوف (wuquf): Standing at Arafat, the essential pillar of Hajj.
حلق (halq): Shaving or trimming hair after the stoning at Mina, a wajib act of Hajj.
افتراق (iftiraq): Physical separation of the spouses after committing a transgression during ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After which stage does sexual intercourse during Hajj require only a sheep (shah) and not invalidate the Hajj according to the Hanafi school?
A) After standing at Arafat
B) After shaving (halq) before tawaf
C) Before standing at Arafat
D) After tawaf al-ifadah

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the effect of intercourse during Hajj/Umrah varies by stage: after Arafat requires a camel, after halq a sheep, and separation is recommendatory, not obligatory.

Muhrim Killing Game: Ruling and Definition
Ruling with definitions and proof for muhrim killing game

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a muhrim who kills game or points it out, explain the definition of sayd, and evaluate the dispute with Ibn Abbas on repeat offenses.

Core Idea
A muhrim who kills game owes a penalty — pointing out game is annexed to killing, and repeat offenses require atonement.

When a person enters ihram for Hajj or Umrah, certain acts become prohibited. Among the most serious is the killing of land game. This lesson unpacks the ruling from the Hanafi school: what counts as game, how pointing out game incurs liability, whether a repeat offender escapes penalty, and why forgetfulness still obligates the penalty (though not sin). We will work through the definition, the annexation of dalalah to qatl, and the refutation of Ibn Abbas's view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The act of a muhrim killing game (sayd). What is the hukm? It is haram (prohibited) and obligates a penalty (wajib).
The penalty is determined by the value of the animal—slaughter equivalent, feed poor, or fast.
2
Define the subject: sayd (game)
Sayd is a wild land animal, untamable by nature, that escapes by wing or legs. Excluded: sea creatures and harmful crawlers (snakes, scorpions).
Sea game is halal for muhrim; land game is haram, even if inedible, except exceptions like crows and predatory animals.
3
Understand annexation of pointing out (dalalah)
If a muhrim points out or indicates game to a killer, and the killing results from that indication while the muhrim remains in ihram, he is liable for the penalty.
No liability if the indication was ineffective (e.g., the killer already knew) or if the kill occurred after the muhrim exited ihram.
4
Examine the dispute on repeat offense
Ibn Abbas held that a repeat offender has no penalty, citing Quran 5:95. The Hanafi school refutes this: the cause (killing game) is the same, so the ruling is the same; the verse threatens divine vengeance, which does not negate worldly obligation.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.
5
Note the ruling on forgetfulness vs. intent
In ihram transgressions, deliberate, accidental, forgetful, and coerced acts all incur the penalty. Sin (ithm) attaches only to deliberate intent.
This ensures consistency in protecting the sanctity of ihram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قتل محرم صيداً ، أو دلّ عليه قاتله بدءاً، أو عوداً، (سهواً، أو عمداً فإن قتل محرم صيداً ، أو دلّ عليه قاتله بدءاً، أو عوداً) : أي سواء كان أَوَّلَ مرَّة أو لا، (سهواً، أو عمداً [١] قوله : صيداً ؛ المراد به الحيوان البري المتوحش بأصل خلقته، الممتنع بجناحه أو قوائمه، فخرج بالقيد الأوّل البحري، وهو الذي يكون توالده في البحر، وإن كان مثواه في البر، وبالقيد الثاني الحيّة والعقرب، وسائر هوام الأرض. فصيد البحر حلال له مطلقاً، ولو كان غير مأكول كالسلحفاة والضفدع، وصيد البر حرام مطلقاً، ولو غير مأكول كالخنزير، إلا ما يستثنى منه ، وهـو الـغـراب وهـو الحدأة، والسَّبُعُ المؤذي ، ونحو ذلك. كذا في «فتح القدير» و«البحر» [٢] قوله : أو دلّ عليه ... الخ ؛ فإنّ دلالة المحرم على الصيد، وكذا الإشارة إليه للقاتل ملحق بالقتل، روي ذلك عن جمع من الصحابة ، كما ذكره الطحاوي ، وهذا إذا حصل القتل بسبب دلالته أو إشارته، وهو باق على إحرامه ، فإن دلّ في إحرامه ووقع القتل حال إحلاله، أو دلّ مَن كان عالماً بموضع الصيد، فلم يؤثر صنعه فيه الاجزاء عليه. كذا في «اللباب»، وغيره. [٣] قوله : بدءاً أو عوداً ؛ صرَّح به نفياً ، لما روي عن ابن عباس أنه لا جزاء على العائد ؛ لقوله : وَمَنْ عَادَ فَيَنتَقِمُ اللَّهُ مِنْهُ . ولنا : أنَّ الموجب للجناية، وهو قتل الصيد وما ينوب منابه لا يختلف، فلا يختلفُ الحكم، ولما كان العود أشدَّ من البدءِ رتَّبَ الله عليه في كلامه انتقامه ، وأكده بقوله : واللهُ عَزِيزٌ ذُو انتِقَامٍ )) ، ولا دلالة فيه على نفي الجزاء فيه. (٤) قوله : سهواً أو عمداً ؛ فإنّ جنايات الإحرام يستوي فيها العمد والخطأ، والسهو والنسيان والإكراه، وغير ذلك، غاية الأمر أن الإثم يختص بالعمد.
For if a muhrim kills game, or points it out to its killer, whether first time or returning, (whether forgetfully or deliberately). (For if a muhrim kills game, or points it out to its killer, whether first time or returning): i.e., whether it is the first time or not, (forgetfully or deliberately). [1] He said: 'Game'; what is meant by it is the wild land animal by its original creation, that is unattainable by its wing or legs. So by the first restriction, the sea animal is excluded, which is that whose reproduction is in the sea even if its dwelling is on land; and by the second restriction, the snake and scorpion and all creeping creatures of the earth are excluded. So sea game is absolutely permissible for him, even if it is not edible like the turtle and frog; and land game is absolutely prohibited, even if not edible like the pig, except what is exempted from it, namely the crow which is the kite, and the harmful predator, and the like. Thus in Fath al-Qadir and al-Bahr. [2] He said: 'Or points it out... etc.'; for indeed the muhrim's indication of the game, and likewise his pointing to it for the killer, is annexed to killing. That is narrated from a group of the Companions, as al-Tahawi mentioned. And this applies when the killing occurs due to his indication or pointing, and he remains in his ihram. If he pointed while in ihram but the killing occurred while he was in halal (out of ihram), or he pointed to someone who already knew the location of the game, and his action had no effect, then no penalty is due upon him. Thus in al-Lubab and others. [3] He said: 'First time or returning'; he explicitly mentioned it to negate what is narrated from Ibn Abbas that there is no penalty for the one who returns (repeats), based on His saying: 'And whoever returns, Allah will take vengeance on him.' Our (Hanafi) view: the cause of the transgression, which is killing game and what takes its place, does not differ, so the ruling does not differ. And since returning is more severe than the first time, Allah attached in His speech vengeance to it, and emphasized it with His saying: 'And Allah is Mighty, Possessor of Vengeance.' There is no indication in that of negating the penalty. (4) He said: 'Forgetfully or deliberately'; for indeed the transgressions of ihram are equal in deliberate and mistake, forgetfulness, forgetfulness, coercion, and other states; the only difference is that sin is specific to the deliberate.
Source pages p. 624

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling for a muhrim (person in ihram) who kills game (صيد) or points it out to someone who then kills it. The ruling applies whether it is the first time or a repeated offense, and whether done forgetfully or deliberately. The text then defines 'game' as wild land animals that are naturally untamable, excluding sea animals (even if they live partly on land) and harmful creatures like snakes and scorpions. Sea game is entirely permissible for the muhrim, land game is entirely prohibited except for certain exceptions like the crow (identified as the kite) and harmful predators. The commentary explains that pointing out game is annexed to actual killing based on reports from Companions, provided the killing results from the indication while the muhrim is still in ihram. If the pointing occurs in ihram but the killing happens after the muhrim has exited ihram, or if the person pointed to already knew the location, then no penalty is due. The passage then addresses the opinion of Ibn Abbas that a repeat offender has no penalty, citing Quran 5:95 ('Whoever returns, Allah will take vengeance'). The Hanafi response is that the cause (قتل الصيد) is the same regardless, so the ruling is the same; the Quranic verse only threatens vengeance, which does not negate the worldly penalty. Finally, it states that in ihram transgressions, deliberate and accidental acts are equal in requiring penalty, but sin is only for the deliberate.

Key Terms
صيد (sayd): Wild land animal that is untamable by nature, excluded sea creatures and harmful insects/reptiles.
محرِم (muhrim): A person who has entered the state of ihram for Hajj or Umrah.
دلالة (dalalah): Indicating or pointing out game to another person who then kills it, making the muhrim liable.
جزاء (jaza'): The penalty for killing game while in ihram, which is slaughtering an equivalent animal, or feeding the poor, or fasting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A muhrim who kills or even points out land game incurs a wajib penalty, regardless of intent or repetition, unless the pointing is ineffective or occurs after leaving ihram.

Penalty for Muhrim Killing Game: Full Hanafi Ruling
Explains penalty for muhrim killing game including directing, repetition, intentionality, and special cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the penalty for a muhrim killing game in detail, including liability for repeat offenses, domesticated game, and predators, and explain the Hanafi response to opposing views.

Core Idea
The muhrim's liability for killing game is based on the act, not intention; even repeats, domesticated game, and predators incur a penalty, capped at a sheep for predators.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya explains the comprehensive Hanafi ruling on the penalty (Jaza) for a muhrim who kills game. It covers the definition of game, the liability for directing, the effect of repetition, and the treatment of various types of game including predators and domesticated animals. The author also refutes opposing views from Ibn Abbas and Malik, establishing the Hanafi position with Quranic proof and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its scope
What triggers the penalty? The muhrim kills game, or directs another to kill it, whether first time or returning, forgetfully or intentionally.
The ruling applies broadly; directing is equated to killing.
2
Define 'game' (sayd) and what is excluded
Game is a wild land animal by original creation that prevents capture by wings or legs. Excluded: sea creatures, harmful pests (snakes, scorpions, etc.). Sea game is permissible land game is forbidden with exceptions.
Domesticated wild animals (musta'nas) retain game status.
3
Analyze liability for repeated offense
The author refutes Ibn Abbas' view that no penalty applies for repeat. The cause (killing game) is identical, so the ruling is identical; the Quranic verse on vengeance does not negate penalty.
The Hanafi rely on the consistency of the cause (علة).
4
Recognize that intent does not affect liability but sin
In ihram violations, intentional and unintentional are equal regarding penalty. Sin is specific to intentional acts. Necessity removes sin but not liability.
This is a key principle: الضمان لا يسقط بالضرورة.
5
Identify the penalty calculation and disposition
Penalty is value assessed by two just men at place of killing; for predators capped at a sheep. The value is used to buy a sacrifice or food in Mecca.
The cap reflects that predator meat is not edible for sacrifice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قتل محرم صيداً، أو دلّ عليه قاتله بدءاً أو عوداً، سهواً أو عمداً. [1] قوله: صيداً؛ المراد به الحيوان البري المتوحش بأصل خلقته، الممتنع بجناحه أو قوائمه، فخرج بالقيد الأول البحري، وهو الذي يكون توالده في البحر، وإن كان مثواه في البر، وبالقيد الثاني الحية والعقرب وسائر هوام الأرض. فصيد البحر حلال له مطلقاً، ولو كان غير مأكول كالسلحفاة والضفدع، وصيد البر حرام مطلقاً، ولو غير مأكول كالخنزير، إلا ما يستثنى منه، وهو الغراب والحدأة والسبع المؤذي ونحو ذلك. كذا في «فتح القدير» و«البحر». [2] قوله: أو دلّ عليه ... الخ؛ فإن دلالة المحرم على الصيد، وكذا الإشارة إليه للقاتل ملحق بالقتل، روي ذلك عن جمع من الصحابة، كما ذكره الطحاوي، وهذا إذا حصل القتل بسبب دلالته أو إشارته، وهو باق على إحرامه، فإن دلّ في إحرامه ووقع القتل حال إحلاله، أو دلّ من كان عالماً بموضع الصيد، فلم يؤثر صنعه فيه، لا جزاء عليه. كذا في «اللباب» وغيره. [3] قوله: بدءاً أو عوداً؛ صرح به نفياً لما روي عن ابن عباس أنه لا جزاء على العائد؛ لقوله: {وَمَنْ عَادَ فَيَنْتَقِمُ اللَّهُ مِنْهُ}. ولنا: أن الموجب للجناية، وهو قتل الصيد وما ينوب منابه لا يختلف، فلا يختلف الحكم، ولما كان العود أشد من البدء رتب الله عليه في كلامه انتقامه، وأكده بقوله: {وَاللَّهُ عَزِيزٌ ذُو انْتِقَامٍ}، ولا دلالة فيه على نفي الجزاء. [4] قوله: سهواً أو عمداً؛ فإن جنايات الإحرام يستوي فيها العمد والخطأ، والسهو والنسيان والإكراه وغير ذلك، غاية الأمر أن الإثم يختص بالعمد. فعليه جزاؤه، ولو سبعاً أو مستأنساً أو حماماً مسرولاً، وهو مضطر إلى أكله، وجزاؤه ما قومه عدلان في مقتله، أو أقرب مكان منه، لكن في السبع لا يزيد على شاة، ثم له أن يشتري به هدياً ويذبحه بمكة، أو طعاماً ويتصدق بمكة. [5] قوله: سبعاً؛ بفتح السين وضم الباء: وهو اسم لكل جارح مختطف منتهب، ويستثنى منه الصائل، فإنه لا يجب بقتله شيء، وكذا ما استثنى في الشرع وجوز قتله. [6] قوله: أو مستأنساً؛ أي ولو كان مستأنساً غير متوحش كالظبي المتسأنس في البيت، فإن استئناسه عارض، والمعتبر هو أصل الخلقة، فلا يخرج باستئناسه عن الصيد. [7] قوله: أو حماماً بفتحتين مسرولاً بفتح الواو؛ هو الذي في رجليه ريش كالسراويل، صرح به نفياً لقول مالك، فإنه لا جزاء فيه عنده؛ لكونه ألوفاً لا يطير بجناحه، وفي السبع خلاف للشافعي، فلذلك صرح به. [8] قوله: وهو؛ يعني وإن كان القاتل مضطراً إلى أكله، فإن الاضطرار يرفع الإثم لا الضمان. [9] قوله: ما قومه عدلان؛ أي غير فاسقين، عارفان بقيم الحيوانات. [10] قوله: أي؛ أشار به إلى أن: «أو» ليست للتخيير، بل للتوزيع. [11] قوله: في السبع؛ المراد به الحيوان الذي لا يؤكل. [12] قوله: على شاة؛ فإن كانت قيمته أكثر لا يجب إلا بقدر ما يشتري به. [13] قوله: بمكة؛ أي بالحرم، ولو خارج البلدة، فلو ذبحه في الحل (لأنه صيد في الأصل، فلا يبطله الاستئناس كالبعير إذا ندَّ لا يأخذ حكم الصيد في الحرمة على المحرم. ينظر: «فتح القدير» 3: 90).
If a muhrim kills game, or directs the killer to it, whether first time or returning, whether forgetfully or intentionally. [1] His saying: 'game' means the wild land animal by its original creation, that prevents capture by its wings or legs. Thus excluded by the first condition: sea creatures, which breed in the sea even if they dwell on land; and by the second condition: snakes, scorpions, and all creeping things of the earth. So sea game is absolutely permissible for him, even if not edible like the turtle and frog; and land game is absolutely forbidden, even if not edible like the pig, except what is excluded from it: the crow (which is the kite), the harmful predator, and similar. Thus in Fath al-Qadir and al-Bahr. [2] His saying: 'or directs...': indeed the muhrim's directing to game, and similarly his pointing it out to the killer, is considered like killing. This is reported from a group of Companions as narrated by al-Tahawi. This applies when the killing occurs due to his direction or indication, and he remains in ihram. If he directs in his ihram but the killing occurs after he leaves ihram, or if he directs someone already knowing the game's location and his action had no effect, then no penalty is due. Thus in al-Lubab and others. [3] His saying: 'first time or returning': he explicitly stated it to negate what is reported from Ibn Abbas that there is no penalty on the one who returns, due to His saying: {And whoever returns, Allah will take vengeance on him}. Our proof: the cause of the violation, which is killing game and its equivalent, does not differ, so the ruling does not differ. Since returning is more severe than the first time, Allah linked vengeance to it in His speech and emphasized with: {And Allah is Mighty, Possessor of vengeance}. There is no indication in this of negating the penalty. [4] His saying: 'forgetfully or intentionally': indeed in ihram violations, intentional and unintentional, forgetfulness, oversight, coercion, and others are equal; the only difference is that sin is specific to intentional acts. So upon him is its penalty, even if it is a predator, or a domesticated animal, or a pigeon with feathered legs, even if he is compelled to eat it. Its penalty is what two just men assess as its value at the place of killing, or the nearest place to it. But for the predator it does not exceed a sheep. Then he may buy with it a sacrificial animal and slaughter it in Mecca, or buy food and give charity in Mecca. [5] His saying: 'predator' (with fatha on seen and damma on ba): it is a name for every predatory beast that snatches and seizes. The attacking one is excluded from it, for nothing is due for killing it, and similarly what the law has excluded and permitted killing. [6] His saying: 'or domesticated': meaning even if it is tame, not wild, like a gazelle that has become domesticated in the house. Indeed its domestication is incidental; the consideration is the original creation, so it does not exit from being game by its domestication. [7] His saying: 'or pigeon (with two fatha) feathered-legged (with fatha on waw)': it is the one with feathers on its legs like trousers. He explicitly mentioned it to negate the statement of Malik, for with him there is no penalty on it because it is domestic and does not fly with its wings. Regarding the predator, there is a disagreement with al-Shafi'i, so he explicitly mentioned it. [8] His saying: 'he is compelled': meaning even if the killer is compelled to eat it. Indeed necessity removes sin but not liability. [9] His saying: 'what two just men assess': meaning not corrupt, knowledgeable about the values of animals. [10] His saying: 'its value': he indicated that 'or' is not for choice but for distribution. [11] His saying: 'for the predator': it means the animal that is not eaten. [12] His saying: 'a sheep': if its value is more, only the amount to buy a sheep is required. [13] His saying: 'in Mecca': meaning the sanctuary, even if outside the town. If he slaughters it outside the sanctuary... (because it is originally game, domestication does not nullify it, just as if a camel goes wild, it does not take the ruling of game regarding prohibition on the muhrim. See: Fath al-Qadir 3:90).
Source pages p. 624

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the penalty (جزاء) for a muhrim who kills game. The author begins by stating the core ruling: if a muhrim kills game, or directs another to kill it, whether it is his first time or a repeated offense, and whether done forgetfully or intentionally, he incurs the penalty. The commentary then clarifies each term. First, 'game' (صيد) is defined as wild land animals that can only be captured with difficulty; this excludes sea creatures (even if they live on land) and harmful insects like snakes and scorpions. Sea game is permissible for the muhrim, land game is forbidden, with exceptions like crows and harmful predators. Second, directing or pointing out game to a killer is considered the same as killing, based on reports from Companions; however, this only applies if the muhrim is still in ihram when the killing occurs and his action caused it. Third, the author refutes the opinion of Ibn Abbas that no penalty applies for a repeated offense by citing the Qur'anic verse (5:95) which mentions vengeance for returning, arguing that the cause (قتل الصيد) is the same, so the penalty remains; the verse only emphasizes the severity of the sin, not the absence of penalty. Fourth, for violations of ihram, intent does not affect liability—only the sin differs. The ruling then states that the penalty applies even if the game is a predator, a domesticated animal, or a pigeon with feathered legs, and even if the muhrim was compelled to eat it; necessity removes sin but not liability. The penalty is the value assessed by two just men at the place of killing or the nearest place; for a predator, the penalty is capped at a sheep. The penitent may then use that value to buy a sacrificial animal and slaughter it in Mecca, or buy food and give charity there. The commentary then explains: 'predator' means any attacking beast, except one that attacks the killer; 'domesticated' means even if tamed, it remains game due to its wild origin; 'pigeon' with feathered legs is mentioned to contradict Malik who exempts it; the phrase 'what two just men assess' indicates a distribution not a choice; the cap on predator reflects that its meat is not edible; and 'in Mecca' includes all of the sanctuary. This passage establishes the Hanafi position with scriptural proof and responses to opposing views.

Key Terms
صيد (sayd): Wild land animal that prevents capture by its wings or legs; excludes sea creatures and crawling pests.
محرم (muhrim): A person in the state of ihram for Hajj or Umrah.
جزاء (jaza'): The penalty/compensation for killing game, assessed as value by two just men, to be used for sacrifice or charity in Mecca.
عدلان (adlan): Two just men, not corrupt, knowledgeable about animal values.
سبع (sabu'): Predatory beast that snatches and seizes; in the ruling it refers to animals not eaten (non-edible predators).
مستأنس (musta'nas): Domesticated wild animal, e.g., a tame gazelle; considered game due to original wild nature.
حمام مسرول (hamam musarwil): Pigeon with feathers on its legs resembling trousers; considered game.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the maximum penalty for a muhrim who kills a predator (سبع) while in ihram?
A) The full value assessed by two just men, with no maximum.
B) A sheep (شاة), regardless of the predator's market value.
C) Nothing, because predators are harmful and can be killed.
D) The value of a camel, as it is a large animal.

Key Takeaway
The muhrim's penalty for killing game is wajib in all cases, regardless of repeat, domestication, or necessity, with the predator capped at a sheep.

The Penalty for Hunting Game in Ihram: Evaluation and Expiation
Ruling on penalty for hunting various animals with evaluation and expiation options

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules for evaluating and expiating the penalty for killing game while in ihram, identifying the maximum for beasts of prey and the conditions requiring expiation.

Core Idea
A muhrim must pay the value of game killed, assessed by two just men, with options to sacrifice, feed, or fast—but sin is removed if compelled.

When a person in ihram kills game, whether a beast of prey, domesticated animal, or a pigeon with leg feathers, they incur a penalty. This lesson examines how the penalty is evaluated and the available expiation methods, based on the passage from Al-Wiqayah and its commentary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is killing game while in ihram. The ruling is that a penalty (jaza') is wajib as compensation.
The penalty is due even if the muhrim was compelled to eat the game; compulsion removes sin but not liability.
2
Identify the animals covered
The text specifies sabu' (beast of prey), musta'nis (domesticated game), and hamam masrula (pigeon with leg feathers). These are considered game even if domesticated or predatory with a cap.
Animals that attack (sailan) or are excepted by Shari'ah are excluded.
3
Determine the evaluation method
Two just men (adlan) appraise the animal's market value at the place of killing or the nearest place where it has value.
The appraisers must be just (non-fasiq) and knowledgeable about animal values.
4
Apply the maximum for beasts of prey
For a beast of prey (sabu'), the penalty does not exceed one sheep, even if the appraised value is higher.
The cap is because such animals are not normally eaten; the penalty is symbolic.
5
Outline the expiation options
With the appraised value, the muhrim may: (a) buy a sacrificial animal and slaughter it in the Haram, or (b) buy food and give charity in the Haram, distributing half a sa' of wheat or one sa' of dates/barley per poor person. Alternatively, he may fast one day per poor person per Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
The fasting option is disputed; see next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعليه جزاؤه، ولو سبعاً ، أو مستأنساً ، أو حماماً مسرولاً ، وهو مضطر إلى أكله، وجزاؤُه ما قومه عدلان في مقتله، أو أقرب مكان منه، لكن في السبع لا يزيد على شاة، ثم له أن يشتري به هدياً، ويذبحه بمكة، أو طعاماً ويتصدق بمكة . فعليه جزاؤه، ولو سَبعاً ) : أي لو كان الصَّيد سبعاً، (أو مستأنساً، أو حماماً مسرولاً ، وهو مضطر إلى أكله، وجزاؤُه ما قومه عدلان في مقتله، أو أقرب مكان منه ) : أي إن لم يكن له قيمة في مقتله يقوم في أقرب مكان من مقتله، يكون له فيه قيمة. لكن في السبع لا يزيد على شاة ، ثُمَّ له أن يشتري به هدياً، ويذبحه بمكة، أو طعاماً ويتصدق بمكة . [١]قوله : سبعاً - بفتح السين ، وضم الباء - : وهو اسم لكل جارح مختطف منتهب، ويستثنى منه الصائل، فإنّه لا يجب بقتله شيء، وكذا ما استثنى في الشرع وجوز قتله. [٢] قوله : أو مستأنساً ؛ أي ولو كان مستأنساً غير متوحش كالظبي المتسأنس في البيت، فإنّ استئناسه عارض، والمعتبر هو أصل الخلقة، فلا يخرج باستئناسه عن الصيد. [٣] قوله : أو حماماً - بفتحتين – مسرولاً - بفتح الواو - ؛ هو الذي في رجليه ريش كالسراويل، صرح به نفياً لقول مالك الله ، فإنه لا جزاء فيه عنده ؛ لكونه ألوفاً لا يطير بجناحه، وفي السبع خلاف للشافعي ، فلذلك صرّح به. [٤]قوله : وهو ؛ يعني وإن كان القاتل مضطراً إلى أكله، فإنّ الاضطرار يرفعُ الإثم لا الضمان. [0]قوله : ما قومه عدلان ؛ أي غير فاسقين، عارفان بقيم الحيوانات. [٦]قوله : أي ؛ أشار به إلى أن: «أو» ليست للتخيير، بل للتوزيع. [۷]قوله : في السبع ؛ المراد به الحيوان الذي لا يؤكل. [۸]قوله : على شاة ؛ فإن كانت قيمته أكثر لا يجب إلا بقدر ما يشتري به. [۹] قوله : بمكة ؛ أي بالحرم ، ولو خارج البلدة ، فلو ذبحه في الحل . على كل مسكين نصف صاع من بُرّ، أو صاع من تمر، أو شعير لا أقل منه، أو صام عن كل مسكين يوماً ، وإن فَضل أقل من طعام مسكين تصدق به أو صام يوماً . هذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف . وأما عند محمد الله والشافعي لا يجزئه. كذا في «النهر».
He is liable for its penalty, even if it is a beast of prey, or a domesticated one, or a pigeon with feathers on its legs like trousers, and he is compelled to eat it. Its penalty is what two just men appraise it at the place of killing, or the nearest place to it. But for a beast of prey it does not exceed a sheep. Then he may buy with it a sacrificial animal and slaughter it in Mecca, or food and give charity in Mecca. So he is liable for its penalty, even if it is a beast of prey: meaning if the hunted animal is a beast of prey. (Or a domesticated one, or a pigeon with feathers on its legs like trousers, and he is compelled to eat it. Its penalty is what two just men appraise it at the place of killing, or the nearest place to it): meaning if it has no value at the place of killing, it is appraised at the nearest place to the place of killing where it has value. But for a beast of prey it does not exceed a sheep. Then he may buy with it a sacrificial animal and slaughter it in Mecca, or food and give charity in Mecca. [1] His statement: 'beast of prey' - with fath on the seen and damm on the ba - it is a name for every predatory, snatching, plundering animal, except the one that attacks, for no expiation is due for killing it, and likewise what is excepted in the Shari'ah and allowed to be killed. [2] His statement: 'or a domesticated one'; meaning even if it is domesticated and not wild, like a gazelle domesticated in the house, for its domestication is accidental, and consideration is given to the original nature, so it does not leave the category of game by its domestication. [3] His statement: 'or a pigeon' - with two faths - 'with feathers on its legs' - with fath on the waw - it is the one that has feathers on its legs like trousers, stated to negate the statement of Malik, may Allah have mercy on him, for there is no penalty for it according to him, because it is familiar and does not fly with its wings. Regarding the beast of prey, there is disagreement with al-Shafi'i, so he stated it explicitly. [4] His statement: 'and he'; meaning even if the killer was compelled to eat it, for compulsion removes sin but not liability. [5] His statement: 'what two just men appraise'; meaning not transgressors, knowledgeable about the value of animals. [6] His statement: 'or'; indicating that 'or' is not for choice, but for distribution. [7] His statement: 'in the beast of prey'; the intended meaning is the animal that is not eaten. [8] His statement: 'a sheep'; for if its value is more, only enough to buy a sheep is owed. [9] His statement: 'in Mecca'; meaning in the Haram, even if outside the town. For every poor person, half a sa' of wheat, or one sa' of dates or barley, no less, or he may fast one day for every poor person. And if there is a remainder less than the food of one poor person, he gives charity with it or fasts a day. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. As for Muhammad and al-Shafi'i, it is not permissible.
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of Al-Wiqayah, expanded by the commentator. It deals with the penalty (jaza') for a muhrim (person in ihram) who kills game. The ruling covers various types: a beast of prey (sabu'), a domesticated animal (musta'nis), a specific type of pigeon (hamam masrula), and even if the killer was compelled to eat it. The penalty is the value assessed by two just men at the place of killing or the nearest place where it has value. For a beast of prey (which is not eaten), the maximum penalty is a sheep. Once the value is determined, the muhrim has options: buy a sacrificial animal with that value and slaughter it in the Haram (Mecca), or buy food and give it as charity in the Haram. The commentary elaborates on each term. 'Saba' refers to predatory animals, but if the animal attacks, no penalty is required. 'Musta'nis' refers to animals that are domesticated but considered game due to their original nature. 'Hamam masrula' is a pigeon with feathers on its legs like trousers; the commentator notes that Imam Malik held no penalty for it, but the Hanafi school disagrees. The phrase 'he is compelled' means that while sin is removed by compulsion, liability for the value remains. The 'two just men' (adlan) must be just and knowledgeable about animal values. The word 'or' in the matn is for distribution (tawzi'), not choice. The maximum for a beast of prey is a sheep; if its value is more, only the equivalent of a sheep is required. Slaughtering 'in Mecca' means anywhere in the Haram. The passage then addresses the food distribution: half a sa' of wheat or one sa' of dates or barley per poor person, or fasting one day per poor person. If the value is less than that, he gives charity or fasts one day. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. According to Muhammad and al-Shafi'i, this is not valid (i.e., the option to fast is not permissible).

Key Terms
سبع (sabu'): A predatory beast of prey that snatches and pounces, except one that attacks a person.
مستأنس (musta'nis): A game animal that has been domesticated, like a gazelle in a house; considered game due to its original nature.
عدلان (adlan): Two just (non-fasiq) men who are knowledgeable about the values of animals.
جزاء (jaza'): The penalty or compensation for killing game while in ihram, assessed by value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the maximum penalty for killing a beast of prey (sabu') while in ihram?
A) Its full market value
B) One sheep
C) Half a sa' of food per poor person
D) Fasting one day

Key Takeaway
A muhrim must pay the value of game killed, assessed by two just men, with options to sacrifice or feed in the Haram, but for a beast of prey the maximum is one sheep.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Fasting as Expiation for Hunting in Ihram
Intra-Hanafi disagreement on fasting as expiation for hunting in ihram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the option to fast as expiation, and apply the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf allow fasting as expiation for the deficit in food; Muhammad al-Shaybani and al-Shafi'i disallow it—the relied-upon view permits fasting.

The passage concludes with an important intra-Hanafi disagreement: when the appraised value of game is insufficient to feed a full poor person, can the muhrim fast instead of giving the missing food? The two senior imams say yes; Muhammad al-Shaybani and al-Shafi'i say no. This lesson examines the dispute and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context of the dispute
The dispute arises when the muhrim opts for food expiation but the appraised value is not enough to provide a full food portion (half sa' wheat) for each poor person that could be fed.
For example, if the value is 1.5 sa' of wheat, he can feed three poor persons fully. But if the value is 1.4 sa', there is a deficit.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
They allow the muhrim to give charity with the remainder or fast one day for each poor person that the full value would have fed.
The text says: 'وإن فَضل أقل من طعام مسكين تصدق به أو صام يوماً'
3
State the position of Muhammad al-Shaybani and al-Shafi'i
They hold that this is not valid (لا يجزئه). Fasting is not a permissible substitute for a partial deficit in food.
The text mentions: 'وأما عند محمد والشافعي لا يجزئه'
4
Analyze the basis of the disagreement
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf see the fasting option as an original alternative; once the muhrim chooses to feed, he may use fasting to cover any shortfall. Muhammad and al-Shafi'i view the food path as independent, requiring full completion in kind.
The commentator does not provide explicit dala'il, but the difference stems from views on istihsan and qiyas.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is the relied-upon view, as it is presented first and later scholars (like the author of al-Nahr) rely on it.
The commentator does not explicitly state tarjih, but the structure implies preference for the two.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of Al-Wiqayah, expanded by the commentator. It deals with the penalty (jaza') for a muhrim (person in ihram) who kills game. The ruling covers various types: a beast of prey (sabu'), a domesticated animal (musta'nis), a specific type of pigeon (hamam masrula), and even if the killer was compelled to eat it. Key Terms
جزاء (jaza'): The penalty or compensation for killing game while in ihram.
إجزاء (ijza'): Validity or sufficiency of an act (e.g., 'لا يجزئه' means 'it is not sufficient/valid for him').

Five Disputes on the Penalty for Hunting in Ihram
Enumeration of five points of ikhtilaf among Hanafi imams and with al-Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five points of disagreement regarding the penalty for hunting in ihram among Hanafi imams and with al-Shafi'i, and explain the Hanafi position on each.

Core Idea
The penalty for hunting in ihram involves five disagreements: value vs. equivalent, choice of expiation, food basis, fasting measure, and arbitrator binding.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah enumerates five points of legal disagreement (ikhtilaf) concerning the penalty (jaza') for a person in ihram who unlawfully kills game. The disputes span both intra-Hanafi differences and cross-madhhab conflicts with Imam al-Shafi'i. Understanding each of these points clarifies the Hanafi methodology of textual interpretation and the operation of 'or' (aw) as a particle of choice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first dispute: value vs. equivalent animal
The first dispute is about the basis of the penalty. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the obligation is the market value of the game at the place of killing. Muhammad al-Shaybani and al-Shafi'i hold that for game with a livestock counterpart resembling it in appearance, the penalty is that specific animal (al-nazir), not its monetary value.
The Hanafi position relies on the generality of 'value' in the verse, while the opposing view prioritizes resemblance in form.
2
Analyze the second dispute: choice of expiation when able to sacrifice/food
The second dispute is whether a muhrim may choose fasting even if he can afford sacrifice or food. The Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad?) says yes, because 'aw' in the verse indicates free choice. Zafar (a Hanafi imam) says no, drawing an analogy to the expiation for a broken oath (kaffarat al-yamin), where fasting is not allowed if one can pay.
This dispute occurs within the Hanafi school itself, with Abu Hanifa and the majority opposing Zafar's qiyas.
3
Examine the third dispute: basis for the food option
If the killer chooses to feed the poor, the Hanafi school says he should buy food with the market value of the game. Al-Shafi'i says the consideration should be the value of the equivalent animal (the nazir), not the game itself.
This follows from the first dispute: the valuation base differs.
4
Study the fourth dispute: measure of food per day of fasting
If the killer chooses to fast, the Hanafi school requires fasting one day for every half sa' (approx. 1.5 liters) of food. Al-Shafi'i requires fasting one day for every mudd (approx. 0.5 liters), a smaller quantity.
The practical difference is significant: the number of days of fasting may differ substantially.
5
Understand the fifth dispute: role of arbitrators and binding nature
The two arbitrators appointed to assess the penalty only determine the value of the killed animal; after that, the killer retains the choice among sacrifice, food, or fasting. According to al-Shafi'i and Muhammad, if the arbitrators specify a particular type (e.g., sacrifice), that type becomes binding on the killer.
The Hanafi position upholds the killer's freedom of choice, while the opposing view gives authority to the arbitrators' specification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصول : [١]قوله : وأما عند محمد الله ... الخ ؛ قال في «النهاية»: الخلاف في هذه المسألة في أحدها : إن الواجب على المحرم القاتل قيمة الصيد في الموضع الذي قتله فيه عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف ، وقال محمد والشافعي له : يجب النظير فيما له نظير من النعم التي يشبه في المنظر لا في القيمة. الثاني : إنه يجوز للمحرم أن يختار الصوم مع القدرة على الهدي والإطعام عندنا ؛ لقوله : أَوْ عَدْلُ ذَلِكَ ) (۲) ، وحرف : «أو» للتخيير، وعند زفـر : لا يجوز له الصوم مع القدرة على التكفير بالمال قياساً على كفارة اليمين. والثالث : إنه إذا اختار الطعام ، فالمختار يشتري بقيمة الصيد، وعند الشافعي: المعتبر قيمة النظير. والرابع : إنه إذا اختار الصيام يصومُ مكان كل نصف صاع يوماً، وعند الشافعي : يصوم مكان كل مد. والخامس : إن الذي إلى الحكمين تقويم المقتول ، فإذا ظهرت قيمته ، فالخيار إلى القاتل، بين أن يشتري به هدياً أو طعاماً أو صام، وعند الشافعي ومحمد : إذا عينا نوعه لَزِمه ذلك النوع.
Sections (Fusul): [1] His statement: 'As for according to Muhammad...'; he said in al-Nihayah: The disagreement in this matter is in five: First: The obligation on the muhrim killer is the value of the game in the place where he killed it according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad and al-Shafi'i said: The equivalent (al-nazir) is obligatory for that which has an equivalent among livestock that resembles it in appearance, not in value. Second: It is permissible for the muhrim to choose fasting while being able to sacrifice and feed according to us, due to His statement: 'or the equivalent of that' (Quran 5:95), and the letter 'or' is for choice. According to Zafar: it is not permissible for him to fast while being able to expiate with wealth, by analogy to the expiation of an oath. Third: If he chooses food, then the chosen method is to buy with the value of the game. According to al-Shafi'i: the consideration is the value of the equivalent. Fourth: If he chooses fasting, he fasts in place of every half sa' one day. According to al-Shafi'i: he fasts in place of every mudd (a smaller measure). Fifth: The task of the two arbitrators is the evaluation of the killed animal; when its value appears, the choice is with the killer, between buying a sacrificial animal with it, or food, or fasting. According to al-Shafi'i and Muhammad: when they specify a type, that type becomes binding on him.
Source pages p. 626

Commentary
This passage is a set of five sections (fusul) detailing the points of disagreement (ikhtilaf) regarding the penalty for hunting. It is from the commentary of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah and cites al-Nihayah. The first dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say the obligation is the market value of the game at the place of killing. Muhammad al-Shaybani and al-Shafi'i say that for game that has an equivalent among livestock (such as a deer comparable to a sheep), the penalty is that equivalent animal, not its value. Second dispute: The Hanafi school (our school) allows the muhrim to choose fasting even if he can afford sacrifice or food, using the Quranic verse 'or the equivalent of that' (5:95) where 'or' indicates choice. Zafar (a Hanafi imam) does not allow fasting when one has the means, analogy to the expiation of an oath. Third dispute: If he chooses food, the Hanafis say he buys food with the value of the game. Al-Shafi'i says the consideration is the value of the equivalent animal (the nazir). Fourth dispute: If he chooses fasting, the Hanafis say he fasts one day for every half sa' of food. Al-Shafi'i says he fasts one day for every mudd (which is smaller than half sa'). Fifth dispute: The role of the two arbitrators is only to evaluate the killed animal. Once the value is known, the choice is with the killer to buy a sacrifice, food, or fast. According to al-Shafi'i and Muhammad, if the arbitrators specify a particular type (e.g., sacrifice), that type becomes binding on the killer. This summarizes the major differences on this issue.

Key Terms
النظير (al-nazir): The equivalent animal from livestock that resembles the game in appearance, used by Muhammad and al-Shafi'i instead of value.
مد (mudd): A measure of volume, approximately 0.5 liters, used by al-Shafi'i for fasting per each mudd of food.
قيمة (qimah): Market value of the game at the place of killing, the basis of penalty for Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling of Zafar regarding the option of fasting for a muhrim who can afford sacrifice or food?
A) It is permissible due to the Quranic 'or' (aw)
B) It is not permissible, by analogy to the expiation of an oath
C) It is not permissible under any circumstances
D) It is obligatory to fast

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school on the penalty for hunting in ihram (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) prioritizes market value, free choice of expiation, and non-binding arbitration, differing intra-school with Muhammad and Zafar, and cross-madhhab with al-Shafi'i.

The Penalty for Killing Game While in Ihram: Dispute over Value vs. Equivalent
Dispute on expiation per poor person and five points of khilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the five points of legal difference between the Hanafi school and other scholars regarding the expiation for killing game while in ihram, and state the Hanafi position on each point.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school grants the muhrim killer full flexibility to choose between value, food, or fasting, based on the choice indicated by 'aw' in Quran 5:95.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya deals with the detailed rulings of expiation for a muhrim who kills game. It first states the basic amounts of food or fasting per poor person, then presents five substantive points of legal disagreement between the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Muhammad al-Shaybani and al-Shafi'i. Understanding these differences is key to mastering the Hanafi approach to penal expiation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first dispute: value vs. equivalent
The first point of disagreement is whether the obligation is the value of the killed game in its location (Hanafi) or an equivalent (nazir) in livestock that resembles the game in appearance (Muhammad and Shafi'i). Note how this foundational difference affects the remaining points.
The Hanafi position relies on the interpretation that 'aw' in the Quran indicates choice, and value suffices.
2
Identify the second dispute: permissibility of fasting when wealthy
The second dispute concerns whether the killer can choose fasting even if he is able to offer a hady (sacrifice) or food. The Hanafis allow it based on 'aw' indicating free choice; Zufar analogizes to oath expiation, which prohibits fasting when wealth is available.
This illustrates intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf (Zufar vs. the majority).
3
Identify the third dispute: basis for food amount
If the killer chooses food, the Hanafis say the amount is determined by the value of the game (buying food with that value). Al-Shafi'i holds that the value of the equivalent is used. This affects the quantity of food to be given.
Practical ramification: the food amount may differ depending on the game's value vs. the equivalent's value.
4
Identify the fourth dispute: conversion to fasting
The fourth dispute concerns the conversion rate: Hanafis say one day of fasting replaces every half sa' of wheat (or equivalent); al-Shafi'i says one day per mudd (a smaller measure). This changes the number of fast days required.
This has a direct impact on the ease or difficulty of fulfilling the expiation.
5
Identify the fifth dispute: binding nature of arbiters' specification
After two arbiters evaluate the game, the killer has the choice to buy a hady, food, or fast according to the Hanafis. According to Muhammad and al-Shafi'i, if the arbiters specify a particular type (e.g., hady), the killer is bound by that specification.
This point affects the final authority over the form of expiation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على كل مسكين نصف صاع من بُرّ، أو صاع من تمر، أو شعير لا أقل منه، أو صام عن كل مسكين يوماً، وإن فَضْلَ أقل من طعام مسكين تصدق به أو صام يوما) هذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وأما عند محمد والشافعي لا يجزئه. كذا في «النهر». فصول : قال في «النهاية»: الخلاف في هذه المسألة في أحدها : إن الواجب على المحرم القاتل قيمة الصيد في الموضع الذي قتله فيه عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف، وقال محمد والشافعي له : يجب النظير فيما له نظير من النعم التي يشبه في المنظر لا في القيمة. الثاني : إنه يجوز للمحرم أن يختار الصوم مع القدرة على الهدي والإطعام عندنا؛ لقوله تعالى: أَوْ عَدْلُ ذَلِكَ، وحرف «أو» للتخيير، وعند زفر : لا يجوز له الصوم مع القدرة على التكفير بالمال قياساً على كفارة اليمين. والثالث : إنه إذا اختار الطعام، فالمختار يشتري بقيمة الصيد، وعند الشافعي: المعتبر قيمة النظير. والرابع : إنه إذا اختار الصيام يصومُ مكان كل نصف صاع يوماً، وعند الشافعي : يصوم مكان كل مد. والخامس : إن الذي إلى الحكمين تقويم المقتول، فإذا ظهرت قيمته، فالخيار إلى القاتل، بين أن يشتري به هدياً أو طعاماً أو صام، وعند الشافعي ومحمد : إذا عينا نوعه لَزِمه ذلك النوع.
For each poor person: half a sa' of wheat, or one sa' of dates or barley, not less than that, or fast one day for each poor person. And if there remains less than the food of one poor person, he gives it in charity or fasts one day. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. As for according to Muhammad and al-Shafi'i, it is not valid. So in 'al-Nahr'. Sections: He said in 'al-Nihaya': The disagreement in this issue is in five points: First: The obligation on the muhrim killer is the value of the game in the place where he killed it according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad and al-Shafi'i said: The equivalent (nazir) is obligatory for what has an equivalent among livestock that resembles it in appearance, not the value. Second: The muhrim is permitted to choose fasting even if able to offer a hady or food according to us, due to His saying: 'or an equivalent of that sacrifice' [Quran 5:95], and the letter 'aw' is for choice. According to Zufar: He may not fast while able to expiate with wealth, by analogy to the expiation of an oath. Third: If he chooses food, then the chosen is to buy with the value of the game, according to al-Shafi'i: the value of the equivalent is considered. Fourth: If he chooses fasting, he fasts for every half sa' a day, according to al-Shafi'i: he fasts for every mudd a day. Fifth: The matter is referred to two arbiters to evaluate the killed game; then once its value appears, the choice is to the killer: either to buy with it a hady, or food, or fast. According to al-Shafi'i and Muhammad: if they specify a type, that type becomes binding.
Source pages p. 626

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute concerning the expiation for a muhrim who kills game. The main ruling from the matn states the amount of food or fasting per poor person: half a sa' of wheat or one sa' of dates/barley, or fasting one day per poor person. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that this is valid, while Muhammad and al-Shafi'i consider it not valid because the obligation is the equivalent in livestock, not food or fasting. The commentary then lists five points of legal difference (khilaf) extracted from 'al-Nihaya'. First: the basis—value vs. equivalent. Second: the permissibility of choosing fasting even if able to offer sacrifice or food; Hanafis cite the word 'aw' (or) in Quran 5:95 as giving choice, while Zufar analogizes to oath expiation. Third: if food is chosen, the amount is determined by the value of the game (Hanafi) or by the value of the equivalent (Shafi'i). Fourth: the conversion to fasting—every half sa' of wheat corresponds to one day of fasting according to the Hanafis, while al-Shafi'i uses a mudd. Fifth: after the arbiters evaluate the game, the killer has full choice between hady, food, or fasting unless the type is specified by the arbiters (Shafi'i/Muhammad). The commentary follows the author's order, first stating the main ruling, then the disagreement on its validity, then the detailed points.

Key Terms
نصف صاع (nisf sa'): Half a sa', a measure of volume; in this context, approximately 1.5 kg of wheat or equivalent.
النظير (al-nazir): The equivalent in livestock (camel, cow, sheep) that resembles the game in appearance (manzar).
مد (mudd): A measure of volume, 1/4 sa' (approx. 0.81 kg wheat).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school gives the muhrim killer a flexible choice between hady, food, or fasting based on the value of the game, unlike the opposing view that requires an equivalent animal and restricts fasting.

Equivalent Animals for Game Killed in Ihram
Ruling specifying animal equivalents for game in ihram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the domestic animal equivalent for each type of game listed and explain the Hanafi ruling that the equivalent is wajib based on value.

Core Idea
For each game animal with a domestic counterpart, a specific equivalent is wajib; the Hanafi school requires buying it with the game's value.

When a muhrim kills game, expiation is required. For game that has a domestic counterpart in form and appearance, a specific animal equivalent is prescribed. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya lists the equivalents for several animals and then clarifies the Hanafi position: those equivalents are to be purchased with the value of the game, not necessarily the exact animal itself. Let's walk through the list, the evidence, and the legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the game animals and their equivalents
Read the list: deer, hyena, hare → anaq (young female goat); jerboa → jafrah (four-month-old goat); ostrich → badanah (camel); wild ass → cow; pigeon → sheep.
2
Understand the meaning of 'mithl' (counterpart)
The commentator explains that 'mithl' refers to resemblance in form and appearance (khilqah wa manzar) among livestock.
3
Examine the proofs (athar and hadith)
The passage cites Ibn Umar's rulings (in Muwatta'), the rulings of Umar, Uthman, Ali for the ostrich, and a marfu' hadith from Jabir about the hyena. These assign specific animals.
These proofs show that the Companions and the Prophet specified equivalents.
4
Analyze the Hanafi interpretation
The Hanafi school (عندنا) interprets these reports as requiring the muhrim to buy the equivalent animal with the value of the killed game, not that the animal itself is directly owed.
This is a key point of divergence from other schools.
5
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
Other schools (especially Shafi'i) may hold that the exact animal is obligatory. The Hanafi view is based on the principle that the real obligation is the value, and the specified animals are merely the maximum that can be spent on expiation.
The practical difference: if a game is worth less than the animal, a Shafi'i might still require the animal; a Hanafi would only require the value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان للصيد مثل صورة يجب ذلك، ففي الظبي والضبع والأرنب عناق، وفي اليربوع جفرة، وفي النعامة بدنة، وفي حمار الوحش بقرة، وفي الحمام شاة تقريباً. [١] قوله : مثل؛ أي ما يُماثله من النعم : الإبل والبقر والشاة في الخلقة والمنظر. [٢] قوله : يجب ذلك؛ عملاً بفعل ابن عمر رضي الله عنه : أنه قضى في الضبع بكبش، وفي الغزال بعنز، وفي الأرنب بعناق، وفي اليربوع بجفرة، أخرجه مالك في الموطأ. وأخرج الشافعي عن عمر وعثمان وعلي رضي الله عنهم أنهم أوجبوا في النعامة بدنة. وفي السنن الأربعة مرفوعاً عن جابر : أنه سأل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن الضبع أصيد هو؟ قال: نعم، ويجعل فيه كبش. وعندنا هذه الآثار محمولة على أنه تشترى بالقيمة هذه الأمثال. [٣] قوله : والضبع؛ بفتح الضاد المعجمة وضم الباء الموحدة : كفتار. [٤] قوله : الأرنب؛ بفتح الهمزة والنون بينهما راء مهملة ساكنة : خرگوش. [٥] قوله : عناق؛ بالفتح : أنثى المعز إذا قويت ما لم تبلغ سنة. والجفرة : أنثى المعز إذا بلغت أربعة أشهر. [٦] قوله : اليربوع؛ بفتح الياء وضم الباء بينهما راء ساكنة : موش وشتى.
If the game has a counterpart in form, that is obligatory. So for the deer (zaby), the hyena (dhab'), and the hare (arnab): a young female goat (anaq). For the jerboa (yarbu'): a jafrah (young female goat at four months). For the ostrich: a camel (badanah). For the wild ass: a cow. For the pigeon: a sheep, approximately. [1] His saying: 'mithl' means what resembles it from livestock: camels, cows, and sheep in form and appearance. [2] His saying: 'that is obligatory' acting upon the action of Ibn Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, that he judged for the hyena a ram, for the gazelle a goat, for the hare an anaq, for the jerboa a jafrah — Malik narrated it in al-Muwatta'. And al-Shafi'i narrated from Umar, Uthman, and Ali, may Allah be pleased with them, that they made obligatory for the ostrich a badanah. And in the four Sunan, raised to the Prophet, from Jabir that he asked the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, about the hyena: 'Is it game?' He said, 'Yes, and a ram is to be offered for it.' According to us, these traditions are interpreted as buying these equivalents with the value. [3] His saying: 'al-dhab' — with fatha on the dad and damma on the ba — is a hyena. [4] His saying: 'al-arnab' — with fatha on the hamza and nun, between them a saakin ra — is a rabbit. [5] His saying: 'anaq' — with fatha — is a female goat if strong, not yet reaching a year. And al-jafrah is a female goat that has reached four months. [6] His saying: 'al-yarbu'' — with fatha on the ya and damma on the ba, between them a saakin ra — is a jerboa.
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof specifying the domestic animal equivalent (mithl) for various wild game that a muhrim might kill. The main text lists the equivalents: for deer, hyena, and hare: a young female goat (anaq); for jerboa: a slightly older female goat (jafrah); for ostrich: a camel (badanah); for wild ass: a cow; for pigeon: a sheep. The commentator then explains that 'mithl' means resemblance in appearance (khilqah and manzar). He provides the dalil: the action of Ibn Umar (Muwatta') and the rulings of Umar, Uthman, and Ali (as reported by al-Shafi'i) for the ostrich, and a marfu' hadith from Jabir about the hyena. The Hanafi school (عندنا) interprets these reports as requiring the killer to purchase these equivalent animals with the value of the game, rather than the specific animal being directly obligatory. The passage concludes with lexical definitions of the animals: dhab' (hyena), arnab (rabbit/hare), anaq (young female goat under one year), jafrah (female goat at four months), and yarbu' (jerboa). The order follows the text: first the list, then the explanation of 'mithl', then the proofs, then the definitions.

Key Terms
عناق (anaq): A young female goat that is strong but has not yet reached one year of age.
جفرة (jafrah): A female goat that has reached four months of age.
بدنة (badanah): A camel (male or female) used as a sacrificial animal.
مثل (mithl): The domestic animal that resembles the game in form and appearance.

Key Takeaway
For game with a domestic counterpart, the Hanafi school requires expiation by purchasing the specified animal using the game's value, not by sacrificing the animal itself.

Interpretation of 'Mithl' in Hunting Compensation: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i/Muhammad
Hanafi refutation of Shafi'i/Muhammad's view on mithl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi interpretation of 'al-mithl' in Quran 5:95 and evaluate the Shafi'i/Muhammad position.

Core Idea
In Quran 5:95, 'al-mithl' means value, not physical likeness—the Hanafi position ensures consistency in compensation.

This lesson examines a key dispute over the meaning of 'al-mithl' (the like) in the Quranic ruling on hunting compensation. The Hanafi school, led by Abu Hanifa, interprets 'al-mithl' as the monetary value of the killed game, while Imam Muhammad and al-Shafi'i contend that a physically similar animal should be sacrificed when possible. The passage presents a robust Hanafi defense of the value-based interpretation, using textual analysis and practical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa holds that 'al-mithl' refers to the value (qimah) of the killed game, used to purchase livestock for sacrifice. This is derived from the verse's phrase 'min an-n'am' meaning 'from livestock'—i.e., the compensation must be from livestock, but not necessarily physically similar.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani and Imam al-Shafi'i argue that where a physical counterpart exists (e.g., a cow for a wild donkey), that specific animal must be sacrificed. They base this on a literal reading of 'al-mithl' as 'the like in form'.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponent cites the word 'mithl' and the phrase 'min an-n'am' to argue that the compensation must be an animal from livestock that resembles the killed game in appearance.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response argues that 'min an-n'am' is attached to an omitted element (the value), not to the physical likeness. Moreover, the verse requires two just judges to determine the compensation, which implies valuation—a process needed for monetary value, not for physical resemblance.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Under the Hanafi view, compensation is always value-based, regardless of whether a physical counterpart exists. Under the opponent's view, specific animals are required for some game (e.g., cow for wild donkey) and value for others (e.g., sparrow). The Hanafi view is more consistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما البقرة فلم تعهد مِثْلَ حمار الوحشي، وكذا البدنةُ للنعامة، وكذا البواقي. فقولُهُ: مِنَ النَّعَمِ : أي كائن من النعم ، فالمعنى أن الواجب جزاء مماثل لما قتله، وهو القيمة كائن من النَّعَم بأن يشتري بتلك القيمة بعض النَّعَم. ثُمَّ قوله : يَحكُمُ بِهِ ذَوَا عَدْلٍ ) يؤيّدُ هذا المعنى، فإنَّ التَّقويم يحتاج إلى رأي العدول، ولولا التقويم أولاً كيف يثبت الاختيار بين النَّعَمِ والكفارة والصيام؟ وأيضاً: لو لم يكن له نظير من النَّعَم ، فعند محمَّدِ والشَّافِعِي) له يُوجِبُ ما يجب عند أبي حنيفة أولاً، فيحملُ المِثْلُ على القيمة ولا دلالة للآية على هذا المعنى. [١] قوله : فقوله : من النعم ؛ جواب عمّا استدلوا به، وحاصله : أن قوله : مِنَ النَّعَمِ لا يدل على أنّ الواجب مشابه المقتول صورة، بل هو متعلّق بمحذوف، وقيل : إنه بيان لما قتل لا للمثل، واسمُ النَّعَم يطلق على الوحشي أيضاً. [٢] قوله : ثم قوله ... الخ ؛ تأييد لمذهبنا من حمل المثل على المثل المعنوي، و حاصله : أنّ الله فوض أمر التماثل إلى رأي الحكمين العدلين المبصرين، وهذا يفيد أن المراد به التماثل المعنوي، فإنّ التماثل الصوري لا سيما إذا أعتبر تقريباً، لا يحتاج إلى رأي العدلين. [٣] قوله : ولولا التقويم ... الخ ؛ هذا تأييد آخر، وحاصله : أنه لو لم يقوّم المقتول أولاً ، بل يراد بالمثل المثل الذي يشبهه خلقة، كيف يصح التخيير بينه وبين الإطعام والصيام؟ فإن معرفة مقدار الطعام وعدل ذلك من الصيام، موقوف على التقويم. (٤) قوله : وأيضاً ... الخ ؛ تأييد آخر لمذهبنا ، وإبطال لمذهب محمد والشافعي ، وحاصله : أنهما إنما يوجبان المثل الصوري فيما يوجد له مثل صوري من أقسام الهدي ؛ أي الغنم والبقر والإبل : كالضبع والنعامة وغيرهما. وما لا يوجد له نظير مشابة كالعصفور، والقمري، والفاختة، وأشباهما، فهما أيضاً لا يوجبان فيه إلا القيمة، مع أنه لا دلالة للآية على هذا التفصيل، من أنه يجب المثلُ الصوري فيما له مثل، والمثلُ المعنوي فيما لا مثل له، بل لا يدلّ إلا على وجوب المثل بأحد المعنيين في الكل. فإذن يلزم فيما اختاراه الجمعُ بين الحقيقة والمجاز إن حمل المثل على كليهما، وحمل الآية على ما تدلّ عليه بخلاف ما إذا حمل المثل على المعنوي، فإنّه مستقيم في الكل من غير كلفة واضطراب.
As for the cow, there is no known example like the wild donkey, and likewise the she-camel for the ostrich, and similarly the rest. So His statement: 'of the livestock' means something from livestock. The meaning is that the required compensation is similar to what he killed, and it is the value, from the livestock, by buying with that value some livestock. Then His statement: 'judges by it two just persons' supports this meaning, because valuation requires the opinion of just persons, and without valuation first, how would the choice between livestock, expiation, and fasting be established? Also: If it had no counterpart from livestock, then according to Muhammad and al-Shafi'i, he would be liable for what is required according to Abu Hanifa first, so they interpret 'the like' to mean value, and there is no indication in the verse for this meaning. [1] His statement: 'His statement: from the livestock' is a response to what they argued, and its summary: that His statement 'from the livestock' does not indicate that the required compensation resembles the killed animal in form, but rather it is attached to an omitted element, and it is said: it is a description of what was killed, not of the like, and the term 'livestock' is also used for wild animals. [2] His statement: 'Then his statement... etc.' is a support for our school of interpreting 'the like' as the conceptual like, and its summary: that Allah delegated the matter of similarity to the opinion of two just, discerning judges, and this indicates that what is meant is conceptual similarity, because formal similarity—especially when considered approximately—does not require the opinion of two just persons. [3] His statement: 'Without valuation... etc.' is another support, and its summary: that if the killed animal were not valued first, but rather the like was intended as the one similar in creation, how would the choice between it, feeding, and fasting be valid? For knowing the amount of food and its equivalent in fasting depends on valuation. (4) His statement: 'Also... etc.' is another support for our school and invalidation of the school of Muhammad and al-Shafi'i, and its summary: that they require the formal like where there is a formal counterpart among the types of sacrifices—i.e., sheep, cows, camels—like the hyena, ostrich, and others. And what has no similar counterpart, like the sparrow, turtledove, ringdove, and the like, they also require only value, even though the verse does not indicate this detail, that the formal like is required where there is a like, and the conceptual like where there is no like, but rather it only indicates the obligation of 'the like' in one of the two meanings for all. Therefore, what they chose necessitates combining the literal and metaphorical if the term 'like' is taken in both senses, and applying the verse to what it indicates is problematic, unlike if 'the like' is taken in the conceptual sense, for it is consistent for all without difficulty or inconsistency.
Source pages p. 629

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the interpretation of the word 'al-mithl' (the like) in Quran 5:95, concerning compensation for hunting in ihram. The Hanafi position, attributed to Abu Hanifa, is that 'al-mithl' means the value (qimah) of the hunted animal, which is then used to purchase livestock for sacrifice in the Haram. The passage begins by affirming that for game animals without a physical counterpart (e.g., wild donkey, ostrich), the only possible compensation is value. The author argues that the verse's phrase 'min an-n'am' (of the livestock) indicates that the compensation should be from livestock purchased with the value, not an animal that physically resembles the killed game. The verse then stipulates that two just persons judge the compensation, which supports the Hanafi view because valuation requires judgment, whereas determining a physical likeness does not. The passage refutes the view of Imam Muhammad and al-Shafi'i, who distinguish between game with a physical counterpart (requiring that specific animal) and those without (requiring value). The Hanafi response argues that the verse does not support such a distinction; rather, 'al-mithl' must be interpreted consistently as value in all cases. The commentary sections [1]–[4] elaborate on the arguments. The author concludes that interpreting 'al-mithl' conceptually (value) leads to a consistent reading of the verse, whereas the opponent's view forces a combination of literal and metaphorical meanings, which is unnecessary and problematic.

Key Terms
المثل (al-mithl): In this passage, 'al-mithl' is interpreted by the Hanafi school as the value (qimah) of the killed game, not its physical likeness.
النَّعَم (an-n'am): Livestock (camels, cattle, sheep) that can be sacrificed in the Haram.
التَّقويم (at-taqwim): Valuation of the killed game by two just persons to determine the monetary compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what does 'al-mithl' (the like) in Quran 5:95 refer to?
A) A physically similar animal from the livestock
B) The monetary value of the killed animal
C) Fasting for three days
D) A random animal chosen by the hunter

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, 'al-mithl' in hunting compensation is consistently interpreted as monetary value, ensuring uniformity across all game animals.
