Compensation for Injury to Game (Daman Ma Naqas)
Rulings on compensation for various injuries to game

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the compensation required for various injuries to game in the Hanafi school and calculate the amount (daman ma naqas) using the part-whole principle.

Core Idea
An injury to game requires compensation equal to the decrease in value; if later killed, the part merges into the whole.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah details the specific compensations for various forms of harm to game animals. Understanding these rulings is essential for pilgrims in ihram and also for those in the Haram area. Let's examine the systematic approach based on the decrease in value and the principle that the part is considered with the whole.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts requiring compensation
List the specific harmful acts mentioned: wounding, plucking hair, cutting a limb, plucking feathers, cutting legs, breaking an egg causing the chick to die. Each necessitates compensation (daman ma naqas).
These are all forms of partial loss or injury short of killing.
2
Understand the method of calculation
The compensation is the difference in the animal's market value before and after the injury. This amount is used to purchase a sacrificial animal or to feed the poor or fast.
The value is assessed as if the animal were sound, then defective.
3
Apply the part-whole principle
If the injured animal is later killed (fully) without having already expiated, only the full value is due; the injury compensation falls away because the part (injury) is subsumed by the whole (killing).
The principle: al-ba'd mu'tabar bi'l-kull (the part is considered with the whole).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب بجرحه ونتف شعره، وقطع عضوه ضمان ما نقص. وبنتف ريشه ، وقطع قوائمه، وكسر بيضه ، وكسره وخروج فرخ ميت. وذبح الحلال [١] قوله : ما نقص ؛ فاعل لقوله : «يجب» ؛ أي يجب بنتف شعرِ الصيد وجرحه ، وقطع عضو منه النقصان، فيقوم صحيحاً ثم ناقصاً، فيشتري بما بين القيمتين هدياً أو يصوم، ولو لم يكفّر حتى قتله ضمن قيمته فقط ، وسقط ضمان الجراحة، كذا حققه في «الفتح». والسر فيه : أن البعض معتبر بالكل، فيجب بتفويت الكل بقتله أو فعل ينوب نتف الريش وغيره مما سيأتي كل القيمة، وفي تفويت البعض بعضه، وهو ما بين القيمتين. [٢] قوله : فرخ ؛ - بفتح الفاء، وسكون الراء المهملة، في الآخــر خــاء معجمة : ولد الطائر. [٣] قوله : وذبح الحلال ؛ قيَّد به لأنّ المحرم ممنوع مطلقاً من ذبح الصيد، صيد حرم كان أو صيد حلّ، وعليه كفارته، بخلاف الحلال ؛ فإنّه يحلّ له صيد الحلّ لا الحرم لحديث : «هي حرام إلى يوم القيامة، لا ينفر صيدها، ولا يخلى خلاها، إلا الإذخر»
And it is obligatory, by wounding it, plucking its hair, or cutting a limb, compensation for what is diminished. And by plucking its feathers, cutting its legs, breaking its egg, cracking it, and the chick dying. And the slaughter by the halal person. [1] His statement: 'what is diminished' is the subject of 'it is obligatory'; i.e., by plucking the hair of the game, wounding it, or cutting a limb, the diminution is obligatory. He values it sound then defective, and buys with the difference between the two values a sacrificial animal or fasts. If he did not expiate until he kills it, he only owes its full value, and the compensation for the injury falls away. This is verified in al-Fath. The secret of it: that the part is considered with the whole; so by causing the loss of the whole through killing or an act that substitutes for plucking feathers and others—all of which will come—the full value is due, and by causing loss of part, a part of it, which is the difference between the two values. [2] His statement: 'chick'—with fatha on the fa, sukun on the ra, and at the end a kha with point: the offspring of a bird. [3] His statement: 'and the slaughter by the halal person': he restricted it because the muhrim is absolutely forbidden from slaughtering game, whether game of the Haram or game of the hill, and upon him is its expiation, unlike the halal person; for he is permitted to hunt game of the hill but not of the Haram, due to the hadith: 'It is forbidden until the Day of Judgment; its game is not to be chased, and its vegetation is not to be removed, except al-idhkhir.'
Source pages p. 630

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings on compensation for various acts that harm game animals, particularly in the context of hunting prohibitions. The text is from the commentary on al-Wiqayah. It opens by stating that wounding, plucking hair, or cutting a limb of game necessitates compensation for the decrease in value (daman ma naqas). Similarly, plucking feathers, cutting legs, breaking an egg (causing the chick to die) also require compensation. The method is to value the animal when healthy, then when injured, and the difference is used to purchase a sacrificial animal or to feed the poor or fast. The commentary (section [1]) explains that 'ma naqas' (what is diminished) is the subject of 'yajib'. The compensation is based on the difference in value. If the hunter later kills the animal without having already expiated, he owes only the full value of the animal, and the previous compensation for the injury falls away because the whole subsumes the part. The principle is: the part is considered with the whole (al-ba'd mu'tabar bi'l-kull). Section [2] defines 'farkh' as a chick. Section [3] discusses slaughter by a halal (non-muhrim) person in the Haram. A muhrim (pilgrim in ihram) is absolutely forbidden from slaughtering any game, whether inside or outside the Haram, and owes expiation. A halal person, however, is permitted to hunt outside the Haram but not inside the Haram, because the Haram is sanctified. The hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) is cited: Mecca is forbidden to hunt and cut vegetation, except for al-idhkhir (a type of grass).

Key Terms
ضمان ما نقص (daman ma naqas): Compensation for the decrease in value of the game due to injury.
فرخ (farkh): The young of a bird, i.e., a chick.
ذبح الحلال (dhabh al-halal): Slaughter by a person not in ihram in the Haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person plucks feathers from a game bird (without killing it), what compensation is required according to this passage?
A) Full value of the bird
B) The difference in the bird's value before and after the plucking
C) Nothing, as long as the bird is not killed
D) A sheep sacrifice

Key Takeaway
Compensation for injury to game is based on the decrease in value; if the game is later killed, only the full value is owed, as the part merges into the whole.

Distinction Between Halal and Muhrim Regarding Slaughter in the Haram
Ruling on slaughter by halal person in Haram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the rulings for a halal (non-muhrim) person and a muhrim regarding hunting and slaughtering game in the Haram.

Core Idea
A muhrim is absolutely forbidden to hunt, while a halal person may hunt outside the Haram but not inside.

This passage also clarifies a key difference between a person in ihram and one not in ihram regarding the sanctity of the Haram. The hadith establishes that the Haram is inviolable. Understanding this distinction is crucial for anyone entering the Haram, whether in ihram or not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage distinguishes between a muhrim (pilgrim in ihram) and a halal (person not in ihram). Note that the context is slaughter of game.
2
State the ruling for the muhrim
A muhrim is absolutely forbidden from slaughtering any game, whether in the Haram or outside it, and owes expiation (kaffarah) if he does so.
The expiation is not detailed here but is understood from other sources.
3
State the ruling for the halal person
A halal person is permitted to hunt and slaughter game outside the Haram but is forbidden from doing so inside the Haram due to its sanctity.
The hadith quoted supports this: the Haram is forbidden to hunt and cut vegetation except al-idhkhir.
4
Apply the rulings
Consider scenarios: a halal person inside Haram sees a deer – he cannot hunt it. A muhrim outside Haram sees a deer – he cannot hunt it either. A halal person outside Haram may hunt.

Liability for Harming Game in the Haram
Commentary on ruling requiring compensation for harming game in the Haram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for compensating harm to game in the Haram, distinguishing between full value and partial diminution, and identify the proof from hadith.

Core Idea
Harm to game in the Haram requires compensation—full value if the animal is rendered helpless, otherwise the decrease in value.

This lesson examines a passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on the liability for harming game within the sacred precincts of Makkah. The commentary expands on the matn of al-Wiqayah, detailing when full value is due versus only the depreciation. We will analyse the ruling, its conditions, and the proof from the Prophet's hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify harmful acts
List the specific actions mentioned in the passage that trigger liability: wounding, plucking hair/feathers, cutting limbs, breaking eggs, killing, etc.
The text divides acts into those that cause partial harm and those that cause total loss.
2
Distinguish between full value and partial compensation
Note that full value (qimah) is due when the animal is rendered incapable of escape (e.g., breaking legs so it cannot run or fly). Partial harm like plucking feathers without rendering helpless requires only the decrease in value (ma bayn al-qimatayn).
This key distinction prevents over- or under-compensation.
3
Understand mithl suri vs. mithl ma'nawi
The passage discusses whether compensation is by physical likeness (e.g., replacing the exact animal) or by notional value. The Hanafi position uses notional value (qimah) for game because exact replacement is often impossible.
This avoids difficulty in combining literal and metaphorical interpretations.
4
Analyse the hadith proof
Locate the hadith cited: 'It is sacred until the Day of Resurrection; its game is not to be chased...' (narrated by the six major hadith compilers). Explain how this establishes the sanctity of the Haram and the obligation of compensation for any harm.
The hadith is ahad, so the ruling is wajib, not fard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب بجرحه ونتف شعره، وقطع عضوه ضمان ما نقص. وبنتف ريشه، وقطع قوائمه، وكسر بيضه، وكسره وخروج فرخ ميت. وذبح الحلال

المثل الصوري فيما له مثل، والمثل المعنوي فيما لا مثل له، بل لا يدل إلا على وجوب المثل بأحد المعنيين في الكل. فإذن يلزم فيما اختاراه الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز إن حمل المثل على كليهما، وحمل الآية على ما تدل عليه بخلاف ما إذا حمل المثل على المعنوي، فإنه مستقيم في الكل من غير كلفة واضطراب.

قوله : ما نقص ; فاعل لقوله : «يجب» ; أي يجب بنتف شعر الصيد وجرحه، وقطع عضو منه النقصان، فيقوم صحيحاً ثم ناقصاً، فيشتري بما بين القيمتين هدياً أو يصوم، ولو لم يكفر حتى قتله ضمن قيمته فقط، وسقط ضمان الجراحة، كذا حققه في «الفتح». والسر فيه : أن البعض معتبر بالكل، فيجب بتفويت الكل بقتله أو فعل ينوب نتف الريش وغيره مما سيأتي كل القيمة، وفي تفويت البعض بعضه، وهو ما بين القيمتين.

قوله : فرخ ; - بفتح الفاء، وسكون الراء المهملة، في الآخر خاء: ولد الطائر.

قوله : وذبح الحلال ; قيد به لأن المحرم ممنوع مطلقاً من ذبح الصيد، صيد حرم كان أو صيد حل، وعليه كفارته، بخلاف الحلال ; فإنه يحل له صيد الحل لا الحرم لحديث : «هي حرام إلى يوم القيامة، لا ينفر صيدها، ولا يخلى خلاها، إلا الإذخر» ، أخرجه مطولاً أصحاب الكتب الستة، فإذا ذبح صيد الحرم تجب عليه

صيد الحرم، وحلبه، وقطع حشيشه وشجره غير مملوك، ولا منبت قيمته

قوله : صيد الحرم ; م ; احترز به عن صيد الحل ; فإنه حلال للحلال.

قوله : وحلبه ; بفتح الحاء المهملة، مصدر مضاف إلى المفعول، وهو الضمير الراجع إلى الصيد ; أي تجب بحلب لبن الصيد قيمته، سواء كان الحالب محرماً أو حلاً، لكن يشترط في الثاني أن يكون لبن صيد الحرم، فإنه لو حلب الحلال صيد الحل، لا شيء عليه، فلا بد من زيادة هذا على هذا التقدير. وإن أرجع الضمير إلى صيد الحرم لم يفهم حكم ما إذا حلب المحرم صيد الحل، وإن جعل المصدر مضافاً إلى الفاعل، ويرجع الضمير إلى الحلال، لم يعلم حكم حلب المحرم، وبالجملة: فمثل هذا الاختصار مخل بلا شبهة.

قوله : وقطع حشيشه ; ضميره وضمير ما يليه راجعان إلى الحرم، والقاطع أعم من أن يكون محرماً أو غيره، والشجر اسم للقائم الذي ينمو، فإذا جف فهو حطب، والحشيش اسم لليابس والرطب، يقال له: العشب، بالفارسية: كياه، والفقهاء يطلقون الحشيش على الكل.

قوله : غير; منصوب على أنه حال ; أي حال كون الحشيش والشجر غير مملوك لأحد من الناس، وغير ما أنبته أحد منهم.

قوله : قيمته ; فاعل ليجب ولا يخفى ما في هذا الاختصار من الانتشار والاختلال، وتفصيل مرامه أنه لو نتف ريش طائر أو قطع قوائمه حتى خرج عن حيز الامتناع بالطيران أو العدو، فعليه قيمة الصيد بتمامه. ولو كسر بيضة للصيد فعليه قيمة البيض، فإن خرج بكسر البيضة فرخ ميت فعليه قيمة الفرخ، ولو ذبح الحلال - بالفتح - وهو غير المحرم صيد الحرم فعليه قيمته
It is obligatory due to wounding it, plucking its hair, cutting its limb, compensation for the diminution. And due to plucking its feathers, cutting its legs, breaking its egg, breaking it, and the exit of a dead chick. And the slaughtering by the non-muhrim. The morphological likeness is for that which has a likeness, and the notional likeness is for that which has no likeness, but it indicates only the obligation of likeness in one of the two meanings in everything. So then it is necessary in what they both chose to combine between the literal and metaphorical if likeness is interpreted in both senses, and the verse is interpreted upon what it indicates contrary to if likeness is interpreted as notional, then it is consistent in everything without difficulty and disturbance. His saying: 'ما نقص' (the diminution) is the subject of 'يجب' (is obligatory); i.e., due to plucking the hair of the game, wounding it, and cutting a limb from it, the diminution is obligatory; so it is appraised sound, then deficient, and one purchases with the difference between the two values a sacrificial animal or fasts. And if he did not expiate until he killed it, he is liable for its value only, and the liability for the wound drops; thus it is verified in 'al-Fath'. And the secret in it is that the part is considered by the whole; so due to causing the loss of the whole by killing it or an act that takes its place, plucking feathers and other than that which will come later, the full value is obligatory; and in causing the loss of a part, a part of it is obligatory, which is the difference between the two values. His saying: 'فرخ' (chick) - with fatha on fa, sukun on ra, and kha at the end: the young of a bird. His saying: 'وذبح الحلال' (and the slaughtering by the non-muhrim): he restricted it because the muhrim is absolutely prohibited from slaughtering game, whether it is game of the Haram or game of the Halal, and his expiation is upon him, contrary to the non-muhrim; for he is permitted game of the Halal, not of the Haram, due to the hadith: 'It is sacred until the Day of Resurrection; its game is not to be chased, its vegetation not to be cut, except al-idhkhir.' It was narrated extensively by the authors of the six books. So if he slaughters game of the Haram, it becomes obligatory upon him game of the Haram, and milking it, cutting its grass, its trees that are not owned, and not the value of what he caused to grow... His saying: 'صيد الحرم' (game of the Haram): he thereby excludes game of the Halal; for it is permissible for the non-muhrim. His saying: 'وحلبه' (and milking it): with fatha on ha, masdar added to the object, and the pronoun refers to the game; i.e., due to milking the milk of the game, its value is obligatory, whether the milker is muhrim or non-muhrim, but in the second [case] it is conditioned that the milk is of game of the Haram; for if the non-muhrim milked game of the Halal, nothing is upon him. So this addition is necessary on this estimation. And if he refers the pronoun to game of the Haram, the ruling on if the muhrim milked game of the Halal is not understood; and if he makes the masdar added to the subject and refers the pronoun to the non-muhrim, the ruling on milking by the muhrim is not known. In short, such abbreviation is clearly defective. His saying: 'وقطع حشيشه' (and cutting its grass): its pronoun and the pronoun of what follows refer to the Haram, and the cutter includes whether muhrim or other. And 'shajar' is the term for the standing that grows, so when it dries it is wood, and 'hashish' is the term for dry and moist; it is called 'ushb' in Persian 'kiyah', and the jurists use 'hashish' for everything. His saying: 'غير' (not): it is in the accusative as a circumstantial accusative; i.e., in the state of the grass and trees being not owned by any of the people, and not of what one of them caused to grow. His saying: 'قيمته' (its value): is the subject of 'يجب' (is obligatory); and it is not hidden what is in this abbreviation of dispersal and defect, and the detail of its intent is that if he plucked feathers of a bird or cut its legs until it left the domain of being unattainable by flying or running, then full value of the game is upon him. And if he broke an egg of the game, then the value of the egg is upon him; and if through breaking the egg a dead chick exits, then the value of the chick is upon him; and if the non-muhrim (with fatha) - i.e., the one who is not muhrim - slaughters game of the Haram, then its value is upon him.
Source pages p. 630

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqayah regarding the penalties for harming game in the Haram. The matn lists several actions that trigger liability: wounding the game, plucking its hair, cutting its limb, plucking feathers, cutting legs, breaking an egg, causing the chick to die, and a non-muhrim slaughtering game in the Haram. The commentator explains that the liability in each case is for the decrease in value (ما نقص), measured by the difference between the sound and damaged values. The discussion of 'المثل' (likeness) addresses whether compensation is by exact equivalent or by monetary value: for items that have an exact equivalent, the equivalent is paid; otherwise, the notional value is paid. The commentator notes that this interpretation avoids the difficulty of combining literal and metaphorical senses. The hadith of the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) regarding the sanctity of Makkah is cited to prove that game in the Haram is inviolable even for a non-muhrim. The commentary then clarifies each term in the matn: 'فرخ' means young bird, 'وذبح الحلال' restricts the ruling to non-muhrim because a muhrim is prohibited absolutely, etc. The commentator also addresses an ambiguity in the wording of the matn regarding milking and cutting grass, noting that the abbreviated text could cause confusion, but the detailed ruling is clear: full value is due when the animal is rendered incapable of flight or rapid movement, while partial harm requires only the depreciation. This ensures that the penalty is proportionate to the damage caused.

Key Terms
المثل الصوري (al-mithl al-suri): The physical or morphological likeness; compensation by an identical item.
المثل المعنوي (al-mithl al-ma'nawi): The notional likeness; the monetary value when an exact equivalent is unavailable.
ضمان (daman): Liability, guarantee, or compensation for harm caused.
حلال (halal): In this context, a person not in a state of ihram (non-muhrim).
صيد الحرم (sayd al-haram): Game (wild animals) within the boundaries of the Haram of Makkah.
فرخ (farkh): The young of a bird, a chick.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a non-muhrim plucks feathers of a bird in the Haram but the bird can still fly, what compensation is required?
A) Full value of the bird
B) Decrease in value (ما بين القيمتين)
C) Value of the feathers
D) No compensation

Key Takeaway
Compensation for harming game in the Haram is wajib; full value is due only when the animal is rendered helpless, otherwise the diminution in value is paid.

Compensation for Harming Game and Vegetation in the Haram
States rulings for compensation due to harming game and vegetation in Haram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for compensation due to harming game and vegetation in the Haram and identify the textual evidence that supports these rulings.

Core Idea
Harm to Haram game or vegetation generally requires monetary value compensation, unless the item is dry or cultivated.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah details the obligations (ḍamān) for damaging protected game and plants within the sacred precincts of Mecca. The rulings are derived from narrations of the Companions, particularly regarding ostrich eggs. Understanding these distinctions is essential for anyone performing Hajj or respecting the sanctity of the Haram. We will walk through the specific cases and their underlying principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What specific actions incur liability? List them: plucking feathers, cutting limbs, breaking eggs, milking, cutting vegetation. For each, determine the hukm level (wajib compensation).
The text uses 'يجب' indicating obligation.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof is cited for these rulings? The text references narrations from Ibn Abbas and Umar, and the hadith of Ka'b ibn Ujrah regarding an ostrich egg.
These are ahad hadith, thus zanni al-thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the proof support the ruling? The command to pay value (قيمته) directly indicates compensation is due for the egg. For other cases, analogical reasoning extends the principle.
The illah (effective cause) is the removal of protection or potential to become game.
4
Note the exceptions and conditions
What are the exceptions? Dried wood (jaff) has no value. Rotten eggs have no value. Cultivated trees (munbit) are exempt. Identify these conditions.
The condition for egg value is that it not be rotten; for trees, it depends on typical cultivation.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Which position is relied upon? The text presents the mufta bihi Hanafi position. Cross-madhhab differences exist but are not detailed here.
The rulings are consistent with the principle that Haram sanctity attaches to wild, unprotected items.

وكلّ شجر ينبت بنفسه، وهو من جنس ما ينبته الناس.
[٤] قوله : مملوكاً ؛ لأحدٍ من الناس ؛ بأن نبت في أرض مملوكة لرجل.
[٥]قوله : لأن مراعاته ... الخ ؛ يعني أن مراعاة الإنبات في حق وجوب الجزاء وعدمه في كل شجر متعذرة، فأقيم كونه مما ينبته الناس غالباً مقام إنباته، والإنبات سبب للملك ، فلم يتعلق به حرمة الحرم، والممنوع إنما هو قطع شجر الحرم لحرمته. [٦]قوله : ففيه القيمة ؛ لكونه ممنوعاً قطعه لحرمة الحرم إلا في قسم، وهو ما نبت
بنفسه، وهو من جنس ما لا ينبته الناس.
Except what has dried [from trees]: that is, it is obligatory [to give] its value for plucking its feathers and so forth. So for plucking feathers and cutting limbs, the value of the game is obligatory because it removes it from the state of being unattainable. And for breaking an egg, the value of the egg is obligatory. And for breaking it while a dead chick emerges, the value of the chick is obligatory. If one milks the milk of game, the value of the milk is obligatory. And if one cuts the grass of the Haram or its tree, the value of what is cut is obligatory upon him. [1] His statement: 'except what has dried' means that which has dried from the tree, so for cutting it there is no liability because it is firewood, not a tree. [2] His statement: 'from the state' - with fathah on the ha' and tashdid on the ya' with kasrah, its origin is 'huyuz', which means side and direction, and it is used here as an extra word like 'zahr' in their saying 'bi-zahri al-ghayb' and 'dhat' in their saying 'dhat yawm'. The conclusion is that plucking feathers and cutting limbs remove the game from its self-protection, and by losing that it is as if one has caused the loss of the whole, so that which is obligatory for causing the loss of the whole becomes obligatory. [3] His statement: 'the value of the egg' - this is narrated from Ibn 'Abbas and 'Umar, recorded by 'Abd al-Razzaq in his Musannaf. It is conditioned that it not be rotten, for if he breaks a rotten egg, nothing is upon him because its liability is not for its essence but for the possibility that it might become game, and that is absent in the rotten one. Thus in al-Fath. (4) His statement: 'alive' means that the chick is valued on the assumption that it is alive, and he gives charity with that. This is if its emergence was due to his breaking. If it is known that its death was due to something other than the break, then there is no liability except for the chick due to lack of guarantee, and nothing for the egg due to its unsuitability for game. Thus in al-Bahr. (1) Because he caused the loss of safety by destroying the means of protection, so he guarantees its compensation. See Durar al-Hukkam (1:248). (2) From Ka'b ibn 'Ujrah: The Prophet judged regarding an ostrich egg that a Muhrim struck, that its value is due. Recorded by 'Abd al-Razzaq, al-Bayhaqi, al-Daraqutni, and Abu Dawud, and its narrators are trustworthy except Musa ibn Hilal, and Ibn 'Adi said: I hope there is no harm in it, as in I'la' al-Sunan (10:420-421), and from Ibn 'Abbas: He said regarding an ostrich egg that a Muhrim struck, its price. Recorded by 'Abd al-Razzaq with a sound chain, as in I'la' al-Sunan (10:427) and others. (3) Fath al-Qadir (3:81). (4) Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (3:36). The value of the milk. His statement: 'and not cultivated' means that it is not something that people plant, and no one planted it, but it grew by itself. So then if it is not owned, he is liable for its value except what has dried. And if it is owned, and someone other than the owner cuts it, then upon him, along with the obligation of that value, there is another value for the owner, whether it is dried or not. And we say: it is not something that people plant, and no one planted it, such that if it were something that people usually plant, then nothing is due whether someone planted it or not, because its being something that people plant is treated as equivalent to planting for ease, since considering actual planting for every tree is difficult. So when it is treated as equivalent to planting, and planting is a cause of ownership, the sanctity of the Haram does not apply to it. And if it is of a kind not usually planted by people, then if someone planted it, nothing is due for what we mentioned, and if no one planted it, then its value is due. [1] His statement: 'the value of the milk' means the milked milk, because it is a part of the game. [2] His statement: 'that is not what people plant' means not of the kind that people usually care to plant, like the trees of umm ghaylan, athl, and other desert trees. (3) His statement: 'and no one planted it' - Al-Kifayah said: Know that the trees of the Haram are of three types: those whose cutting and use is permissible without penalty, and one type whose cutting is not permissible, and if one cuts it, then a penalty is due. As for the three: every tree that people planted and it is of a kind that people plant; every tree that people planted and it is not of a kind that people plant; and every tree that grows by itself and it is of a kind that people plant. [4] His statement: 'owned' means owned by someone, such as having grown on land owned by a man. [5] His statement: 'because considering it...' means that considering actual planting for each tree in terms of obligation and non-obligation of penalty is difficult, so its being something that people usually plant is treated as equivalent to its planting, and planting is a cause of ownership, so the sanctity of the Haram does not attach to it. What is prohibited is cutting the trees of the Haram due to its sanctity. [6] His statement: 'then its value is due' because its cutting is prohibited due to the sanctity of the Haram except in one category: that which grows by itself and is of a kind that people do not plant.
Source pages p. 632

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentary on al-Wiqayah regarding compensation (damān) for harming game (sayd) and vegetation (nabāt) in the Haram. The author first addresses game: plucking feathers or cutting limbs of game removes its ability to escape, so the full value of the game is owed. Breaking an egg requires the egg's value, and if a dead chick emerges, the chick's value. Milking game requires the milk's value. For vegetation, cutting grass or trees in the Haram generally requires the value of what is cut, except dry wood (jaff) which is not considered protected vegetation. The commentary explains key terms and conditions. For eggs, a proof is cited from Ibn Abbas and Umar (may Allah be pleased with them) that the Muhrim owes the value of an ostrich egg. However, a rotten egg has no value because it cannot become game. Similarly, if the chick dies due to something other than the break, only the chick's value is due. For trees, the ruling depends on whether the tree is of a type typically planted by people. If it is, then no liability because it is treated as owned property, not protected by the sanctity of the Haram. If it is not typically planted, and no one planted it, then its value is due. If it is owned (e.g., on private land) and someone else cuts it, the cutter must pay both the Haram penalty and the owner's value.

Key Terms
صيد (sayd): Game animal that is hunted; here it includes all wild animals that are permissible to hunt outside the Haram.
حرم (haram): The sacred precincts of Mecca; special rules apply regarding hunting and cutting vegetation.
قيمة (qīmah): Monetary value or compensation equivalent to the market price of the item.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability or obligation to compensate for loss or damage.
منبت (munbit): Something that is planted or grown by human effort; in this context, it refers to cultivated plants.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for cutting a tree in the Haram that is of a type not usually planted by people, and no one planted it?
A) No compensation is due.
B) The value of the tree must be paid as compensation.
C) The cutter must slaughter a sheep as penalty.
D) Only the owner of the land receives compensation.

Key Takeaway
Compensation for harming game and vegetation in the Haram is generally wajib, but exceptions exist for dried wood and cultivated plants.

Four Categories of Haram Trees and Compensation Rules
Sharh of the ruling on cutting Haram trees

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any tree in the Haram into one of four categories and determine whether compensation is due for cutting it.

Core Idea
Compensation for cutting Haram trees is only required for self-grown, uncultivated, unowned trees.

This passage from the sharh explains the Hanafi ruling on cutting trees in the Haram. The commentator clarifies a dense phrase from the matn by breaking down trees into categories based on whether they are planted by humans or grow naturally, and whether they belong to a type that people normally cultivate. Understanding these categories is essential for determining when cutting a Haram tree incurs a financial obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key phrase
The passage begins by explaining the phrase 'ولا منبت' — 'and it is not planted'. Determine what this phrase negates and how it sets the stage for the classification.
The term 'munbit' is defined as a tree planted by someone.
2
Understand the substitution reasoning
The commentator explains that if a tree belongs to a type that people normally plant, no compensation is due even if no one planted it. This is because the category substitutes for actual planting, making the tree akin to owned property.
The principle: 'kawnuhu mimmā yunbituhu al-nās uqīma maqām al-inbāt' (being of a planted type is treated as actual planting).
3
List the four categories
From the text, extract the four possible combinations: (1) planted by a person and of a planted type, (2) planted by a person and not of a planted type, (3) self-grown and of a planted type, (4) self-grown and not of a planted type.
Reference the al-Kifaya quote in the passage.
4
Determine compensation for each
Apply the rules: categories 1-3 carry no compensation; only category 4 (self-grown, not of a planted type, and not owned) requires compensation. If the tree is owned, an additional value is owed to the owner.
Compensation is wajib as a financial indemnity for violating the sanctity of the Haram.
5
Analyze the role of ownership
The restriction 'unowned' in the matn does not exclude owned trees from Haram compensation; rather, it shows that even if owned, the cutter still owes the Haram value (plus the owner's value).
The 'adami al-mamlūkiyyah' is not for exclusion but to clarify the dual obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيمة اللبن". قوله : ولا مُنبَت : أي ليس مما يُنبتُهُ النَّاس " ، ولم يُنبته أحد بل نبت بنفسه ، فحينئذ إن لم يكن مملوكاً فعليه قيمته إلا ما جَفٌ، وإن كان مملوكاً ، وقد قطعه غيرُ المالك ، فعليه مع وجوب تلك القيمة قيمةٌ أُخْرَى للمالك، سواءٌ جَفَّ أو لا. وإنما قلنا : إنه ليس مما يُنبتُهُ النَّاس، ولم يُنبته أحدٌ حَتَّى لو كان مما يُنبتُهُ النَّاسُ عادة، فلا شيء فيه سواء أنبتَهُ إنسان أو لا ؛ لأن كونَهُ ما يُنبتُهُ النَّاسِ أقيم مقام الإنباتِ تيسيراً ؛ لأنَّ مراعاته في كلّ شجرة متعذرة ، فإذا أقيمَ مُقَامَ الإنبات، والإنبات سبب للملك ، فلم يتعلق به حرمة الحرم. وإن كان مما لا يُنبتُهُ النَّاس عادة، فإن أنبتَهُ إنسانٌ فلا شيء فيه لما ذكرنا ، وإن لم يُنبته إنسان ففيه القيمة". [1] قوله : قيمة اللبن ؛ أي المحلوب ؛ لأنه جزء من أجزاء الصيد. [٢] قوله : أي ليس مما ينبته الناس ؛ أي ليس من جنس ما يهتم الناس بإنباته عادة، وهذا كأشجار أم غيلان، والأثل وغيرهما من أشجار الصحارى. (۳) قوله : ولم ينبته أحد ؛ قال في «الكفاية»: اعلم أن شجر الحرم أنواع ثلاث منها يحلّ قطعها والانتفاع بها من غير جزاء، وواحد منها لا يحلّ قطعها، وإذا قطعها فعليه الجزاء، أما الثلاث : فكل شجر أنبته الناس : وهو من جنس ما ينبته الناس. وكل شجر أنبته الناس، وهو ليس من جنس ما ينبته الناس. وكلّ شجر ينبت بنفسه، وهو من جنس ما ينبته الناس. [٤] قوله : مملوكاً ؛ لأحدٍ من الناس ؛ بأن نبت في أرض مملوكة لرجل. [٥]قوله : لأن مراعاته ... الخ ؛ يعني أن مراعاة الإنبات في حق وجوب الجزاء وعدمه في كل شجر متعذرة، فأقيم كونه مما ينبته الناس غالباً مقام إنباته، والإنبات سبب للملك ، فلم يتعلق به حرمة الحرم، والممنوع إنما هو قطع شجر الحرم لحرمته. [٦]قوله : ففيه القيمة ; لكونه ممنوعاً قطعه لحرمة الحرم إلا في قسم، وهو ما نبت بنفسه، وهو من جنس ما لا ينبته الناس. فعُلِمَ من هذا أن الأقسام أربعة، ولا قيمة إلا في قسم واحد. وعُلِمَ أيضاً : أن التقييد بعدم الإنباتِ ذُكِرَ لإفادة نفي الحكم عما عداه، كما ذكرنا، لكن التقييد بعدم المملوكية لم يذكر لإفادة هذا المعنى؛ إذ في صورة وجوب القيمة لو كان مملوكاً، فتلك القيمة واجبة مع أنه تجب قيمة أُخْرَى للمالك؛ بل ليفيد أنَّ هذا الضمان واجب لا غير؛ بسبب تعلق حرمة الحرم.
Value of milk. His statement: 'and it is not planted': meaning it is not what people plant, and no one planted it, rather it grew on its own. So then if it is not owned, he must pay its value, except what has dried. And if it is owned, and someone other than the owner cut it, then along with the obligation of that value, he owes another value to the owner, whether dried or not. And we say: It is not what people plant, and no one planted it, such that if it were what people usually plant, then there is no penalty whether a person planted it or not, because treating it as what people plant is substituted for actual planting for ease; because considering actual planting for each tree is difficult. So when it is treated as planting, and planting is a cause for ownership, then the sanctity of the Haram does not apply to it. And if it is not what people usually plant, then if a person planted it, there is no penalty due to what we mentioned; but if no one planted it, then there is value. (1) His statement: 'value of milk': meaning the milked milk, because it is part of the game. (2) His statement: 'meaning not what people plant': meaning it is not of the type that people care to plant normally, like trees of Umm Ghaylan, tamarisk, and other desert trees. (3) His statement: 'and no one planted it': He said in al-Kifaya: Know that the trees of the Haram are of three types: some it is permissible to cut and benefit from without penalty, and one type it is not permissible to cut, and if cut then penalty. As for the three: every tree that a person planted and is of a type that people plant; every tree that a person planted and is not of a type that people plant; and every tree that grows on its own and is of a type that people plant. (4) His statement: 'owned': by any person, meaning it grew on a land owned by a man. (5) His statement: 'because considering it...' etc.: means that considering actual planting concerning the obligation or non-obligation of penalty for every tree is difficult, so treating it as what people predominantly plant is made a substitute for its planting, and planting is a cause for ownership, so the sanctity of the Haram does not attach to it, and what is prohibited is cutting the trees of the Haram due to its sanctity. (6) His statement: 'so there is value in it': because cutting it is prohibited due to the sanctity of the Haram except in one category, which is what grows on its own and is of a type that people do not plant. It is understood from this that the categories are four, and there is no value except in one category. And it is also understood that the restriction by 'not planted' was mentioned to negate the ruling for other than it, as we mentioned, but the restriction by 'not owned' was not mentioned to convey this meaning; for in the case of obligation of value, if it were owned, then that value is obligatory while another value is owed to the owner; rather it is to convey that this guarantee is obligatory without more, due to the attachment of the sanctity of the Haram.
Source pages p. 633

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) of the matn's ruling on cutting trees in the Haram. The commentator clarifies the term 'ولا منبت' (not planted) by explaining that it refers to trees that are not of the type people normally cultivate and that no one has actually planted. If such a tree is not owned, cutting it requires compensation (قيمة). If it is owned, the cutter must pay both the compensation for violating the sanctity of the Haram and a separate indemnity to the owner. The commentator then explains that if a tree is of a type people normally plant, no compensation is due regardless of whether someone planted it, because the category substitutes for actual planting, making it akin to owned property. If the tree is not of a type people plant but someone planted it, again no compensation. Only if it is neither planted nor of a planted type is compensation due. The commentator references al-Kifaya to outline four categories of Haram trees, concluding that compensation is only required in one: self-grown trees not of a cultivated type. The final part notes that the matn's restriction 'without planting' serves to exclude other categories, while the restriction 'unowned' is not for exclusion but to show that even if owned, the Haram sanctity imposes an additional obligation.

Key Terms
منبت (munbit): A tree planted by someone, as opposed to self-grown.
قيمة (qimah): Monetary compensation equal to the market value of the tree at the time of cutting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) A tree not of a type people plant, not planted by anyone, and not owned.

Key Takeaway
Compensation for cutting Haram trees is wajib only for self-grown trees not of a cultivated type and not owned; all other categories are exempt.

No Fasting in Four Haram Offenses
States that fasting cannot substitute for compensation in four specific Haram offenses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four cases where fasting cannot substitute for compensation in Haram offenses and explain the legal rationale distinguishing gharamah from kaffarah.

Core Idea
Compensation for harming the Haram's game, eggs, grass, or trees is a fine, not expiation — fasting cannot replace it.

During Hajj or 'Umrah, various violations require compensation. Some can be expiated by fasting, but in four specific cases — slaughtering game, taking eggs, cutting grass, and cutting trees — the Hanafi school holds that only monetary compensation (gharamah) is accepted. Why? The obligation is tied to the object itself, not the act, making it akin to property damage.

Guided Path
1
List the Four Cases
Identify the four offenses: slaughtering game of the Haram, taking its eggs, cutting its grass, and cutting its trees.
These are explicitly mentioned in the source text.
2
State the Ruling
In all four cases, fasting is not a valid substitute; the pilgrim must pay the compensation (gharamah).
This is the mufta bihi position of the Hanafi school.
3
Understand the Rationale (Talil)
The compensation is based on the object (game, grass, tree) — thus it is a fine for property, not an expiation for the act. Fasting cannot replace property compensation.
The text says: 'because the guarantee is based on the object, so it becomes like a guarantee for property.'
4
Contrast with Other Cases
In earlier cases (e.g., hunting for sport), the penalty is for the act, so fasting is suitable as expiation.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the scope of the rule.
5
Apply to Similar Scenarios
Any offense where the harm is directly to a protected object in the Haram (e.g., uprooting a tree) follows the same ruling: gharamah, not fasting.
Use the same test: is the penalty on the object or the act?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ولا صوم في الأربعة ) : أي لا صومَ في ذبح صيد الحرمِ وحليه، وقطع حشيشه وشجره. [1] قوله : ولا صوم في الأربعة ; وذلك لأنّ الجزاء فيها غرامة لا كفارة ; فإنّ الضمان فيه باعتبار المحلّ : وهو الصيد والحشيش والشجر، فصار كغرامة الأموال، بخلاف الضمان في الصور السابقة، فإنّه هناك جزاء الفعل لا المحلّ، والصوم يصلح له ; لأنه كفّارة. كذا في «البحر» . (١) وهي: الأول : مما ينبته الناس عادة، وأنبته إنسان. والثاني مما ينبته الناس، ولم ينبته إنسان. والثالث : مما ينبته الناس، وأنبته إنسان. والرابع : وهو ليس مما ينبته الناس، ونبت بنفسه.
(And there is no fasting in the four): meaning no fasting in slaughtering the game of the Haram, taking its eggs, cutting its grass, and its trees. [1] His statement: there is no fasting in the four; and that is because the penalty in them is a fine, not an expiation; for the guarantee is based on the object: which is the game, grass, and trees, so it becomes like a guarantee for property, unlike the guarantee in the previous cases, for there the penalty is for the act, not the object, and fasting is suitable for that because it is an expiation. As in al-Bahr. (1) They are: the first: which people plant customarily and a person planted it; the second: which people plant and no one planted it; the third: which people plant and a person planted it; the fourth: which is not what people plant and grew on its own.
Source pages p. 634

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling from the matn that no fasting is accepted as a substitute for the prescribed compensation in four cases related to Haram offenses. The commentator clarifies that these four cases are: slaughtering game, taking eggs, cutting grass, and cutting trees. The reason is that the compensation here is a financial fine (gharamah) based on the value of the object (the game, grass, tree), not an expiation for the act. Therefore, it resembles a property guarantee that cannot be replaced by fasting. In contrast, previous cases (like hunting for sport) have penalties tied to the act itself, which can be expiated by fasting. The footnote enumerates the four categories of trees from the earlier discussion, showing that even in the fourth category (non-cultivated, self-grown), the penalty is still a fine, not expiation.

Key Terms
غرامة (gharamah): A financial fine imposed as compensation for harming property or the sanctity of the Haram in these four cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In four specific Haram offenses, the compensation is a financial fine on the object, not an expiation for the act, so fasting is never accepted as a substitute.

No Fasting Expiation for Cutting Haram Plants
Commentary explaining no fasting expiation for cutting plants in Haram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four categories of plants in the Haram and explain why fasting (sawm) is not an acceptable expiation for cutting them, citing the distinction between gharamah and kaffarah.

Core Idea
Cutting Haram plants incurs a financial fine, not a fasting expiation, because the penalty is for damaging the location's sanctity.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya clarifies the Hanafi ruling on penalties for cutting plants in the Haram. The commentator establishes a fourfold classification of plants and explains why fasting is never an option. Instead, the penalty is a monetary fine (gharamah), with a specific value (qima) due only in one category. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the rules of Hajj and the sanctity of the Haram.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four Categories
List the four categories based on whether people normally plant them and whether a human actually planted them. The first category is what people normally plant and a human planted; the second is what people plant but no human planted; the third is what people plant and a human planted (repeated? Actually the text says three: first, second, third, and fourth: not of what people plant and grew by itself). Confirm the exact classification from the source.
The categories are: (1) people normally plant and a human planted; (2) people normally plant but no human planted; (3) people do not normally plant but a human planted; (4) people do not normally plant and grew by itself.
2
Determine Which Category Requires Value (Qima)
From the text 'wa la qimata illa fi qism wa hid', only the first category requires payment of value. Understand that this is the only case where a specific monetary value is due.
The other three categories still incur a gharamah, but the amount is not specified as qima in the text.
3
Understand the Rationale: Gharamah vs Kaffarah
The commentary explains that no fasting is allowed in any of the four categories because the recompense is a fine (gharamah) based on the location (the game, grass, trees), not an expiation (kaffarah) for the act. Fasting is suitable for expiation of acts, but here the damage is to property of the Haram.
The distinction hinges on whether the penalty is attached to the act (fi'l) or the location (mahall).
4
Distinguish from Cases Where Fasting is Valid
Recall that in other contexts like hunting game while in Ihram, fasting can be an expiation. Compare the rationale: there the penalty is for the act of hunting itself, not for the location.
This contrast helps solidify the rule: when the guarantee is for the property, it's a fine; when for the act, it's an expiation.
5
Apply to a Scenario
Given a scenario of a person cutting a tree in the Haram that grew wild (category 4), determine the penalty: no fasting, but a gharamah must be paid. The specific value (qima) is not due because it's not category 1.
This step tests application of the classification and ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلم من هذا أن الأقسام أربعة ولا قيمة إلا في قسم واحد. وعلم أيضا أن التقييد بعدم الإنبات ذكر لإفادة نفي الحكم عما عداه كما ذكرنا لكن التقييد بعدم المملوكية لم يذكر لإفادة هذا المعنى إذ في صورة وجوب القيمة لو كان مملوكا فتلك القيمة واجبة مع أنه تجب قيمة أخرى بل ليفيد أن هذا الضمان واجب لا غير بسبب تعلق حرمة الحرم. ولا صوم في الأربعة أي لا صوم في ذبح صيد الحرم وحليه وقطع حشيشه وشجره. (ثم ذكر الأقسام الأربعة) وهي: الأول: مما ينبته الناس عادة وأنبته إنسان. الثاني: مما ينبته الناس ولم ينبته إنسان. الثالث: مما ينبته الناس وأنبته إنسان. الرابع: وهو ليس مما ينبته الناس ونبت بنفسه. It is known from this that the divisions are four, and value is only in one division. It is also known that the restriction by non-growth was mentioned to convey negation of the ruling for what is other than it, as we mentioned, but the restriction by non-ownership was not mentioned to convey this meaning, because in the case where value is obligatory if it were owned, that value is obligatory while another value is due; rather it is to convey that this guarantee is obligatory only due to the sanctity of the Haram. 'And no fasting in the four' meaning no fasting in slaughtering the game of the Haram, its sweet basil, and cutting its grass and trees. (Then he mentioned the four divisions): First: what people normally plant and a human planted. Second: what people plant but no human planted. Third: what people plant and a human planted. Fourth: what is not of what people plant and grew by itself. (Then he said) 'And no fasting in the four' because the recompense in them is a fine, not an expiation; for the guarantee there is based on the location (the game, grass, and trees), so it became like a fine for property, unlike the guarantee in previous cases, for there it is a recompense for the act, not the location, and fasting is suitable for it because it is an expiation. Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on penalties for violating the Haram's plants. The author establishes four categories of plants based on whether they are typically cultivated by people and whether a human actually planted them. The key ruling is that fasting (sawm) is not an expiation for cutting or damaging these plants; instead, only monetary value (qima) is due, and only in one of the four categories. The reasoning is that the penalty here is a guarantee for the property itself (the plant, game, etc.), not for the act of violation. In contrast, previous cases involved penalties for actions like hunting, where fasting is appropriate as expiation. This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh: property-related violations require compensation, not spiritual expiation.

Key Terms
قيمة (qīmah): Monetary compensation or value paid as penalty for damaging protected property in the Haram.
غرامة (gharāmah): A fine or financial penalty, as opposed to a spiritual expiation.
الأقسام الأربعة (al-aqsām al-arba‘ah): The four categories of plants in the Haram based on cultivation and human agency.

Key Takeaway
Cutting plants in the Haram incurs a financial penalty (gharamah) not a fasting expiation; only the first category of plants requires payment of value (qima).

Grazing and Cutting in Haram: The Idhkhir Exception and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on grazing; hadith-based exception for idhkhir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on grazing and cutting plants in the Haram, explain the idhkhir exception, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
The Haram's plant life is inviolable; only idhkhir is exempted.

The sanctity of the Haram extends to its vegetation. This lesson examines the general prohibition on grazing and cutting, the prophetic exception for idhkhir, and a key disagreement within the Hanafi school on grazing due to hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The text states that grass in the Haram is not to be grazed nor cut, except idhkhir. Recognize this as a universal prohibition with a single exception.
2
Understand the exception for idhkhir
The Prophet ﷺ exempted idhkhir when companions noted its use for graves and houses. This explicit permission indicates that cutting idhkhir is permissible, implying prohibition for all other plants.
Dalil: hadith (Sunnah ahad)
3
Analyze the qiyas argument of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
They analogize animal grazing (cutting with lips) to cutting with tools. Since bringing grass from outside is possible, there is no necessity for grazing inside the Haram.
Qiyas: cutting with mouth = cutting with tools
4
Contrast with Abu Yusuf's istihsan argument
Abu Yusuf permits grazing due to the great hardship of repeatedly going out to bring grass or moving animals out and back. Istihsan overrides qiyas here.
Istihsan based on al-haraj (extreme difficulty)
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Under Abu Hanifa/Muhammad, animals cannot graze inside the Haram; under Abu Yusuf, it is permitted to avoid hardship. The dominant view is prohibition.
Tarjih leans toward Abu Hanifa and Muhammad

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يرعى الحشيش ولا يقطع إلا الإذخر وبقتل قملة أو جرادة صدقة وإن قلت. (قوله ولا يرعى بصيغة المجهول هذا عند محمد وأبي حنيفة بناء على أن القطع بالمشافر كالقطع بالمناجل وحمل الحشيش من الحل ممكن فلا ضرورة إلى الرعي بخلاف الإذخر لأنه استثناه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم حين قال بعض أصحابه إنه لقبورنا وبيوتنا فيجوز قطعه ورعيه. وعند أبي يوسف يجوز الرعي لأن الاحتياج إليه فوق الاحتياج إلى الإذخر في خروج الرعاة إلى الحل ثم عودهم إليه أو حمل الحشيش منه كل مرة حرج عظيم. كذا في البرهان وغيره. قوله ولا يقطع بصيغة المجهول أي لا يجوز قطع حشيش الحرم لا لمحرم ولا لغيره. قوله إلا الإذخر بكسر الهمزة والخاء المعجمة بينهما ذال معجمة ساكنة نبت طيب الرائحة ببلاد الحجاز يسقف به البيوت بين الخشبات ويسد به الخلل في القبور بين اللبنات. قوله قملة بالفتح هو ما يتولد في ثياب الإنسان وشعره باجتماع الوسخ. قوله أو جرادة بالفتح بالفارسية ملخ. قوله صدقة فإن كثر كما إذا قتل ما زاد على ثلاثة قملا كان أو جرادا فالواجب نصف صاع. كذا في البحر. قوله وإن قلت أي وإن كانت قليلة ككف من طعام وقد روي عن عمر رضي الله عنه.
And the grass is not grazed, nor is it cut except idhkhir. And for killing a louse or a locust, charity, even if little. (His statement: 'and is not grazed' in passive form – this is according to Muhammad and Abu Hanifa, based on the view that cutting with the lips is like cutting with scythes, and bringing the grass from outside the Haram is possible, so there is no need for grazing, unlike idhkhir because the Messenger of Allah – peace be upon him – exempted it when some of his companions said it is for our graves and houses, so its cutting and grazing are permissible. According to Abu Yusuf, grazing is permissible because the need for it is greater than the need for idhkhir, as the goatherds going out to the outer area and then returning to it or bringing the grass from there each time is a great hardship. Thus in al-Burhan and others. His statement: 'and is not cut' in passive form means it is not permissible to cut the grass of the Haram, neither for a pilgrim nor for others. His statement: 'except idhkhir' – with kasra on the hamza, and khā' with a dot, between them dhāl with a dot and sukun – is a fragrant plant in the Hijaz region used for roofing houses between beams and for filling gaps in graves between bricks. His statement: 'louse' with fatha is what originates in a person's clothing and hair due to accumulation of dirt. His statement: 'or locust' with fatha, in Persian: malakh. His statement: 'charity' – if many, as when one kills more than three, whether lice or locusts, then the obligatory amount is half a sā‘. Thus in al-Bahr. His statement: 'even if little' means even if it is a small amount, like a handful of food, and it is narrated from Umar – may Allah be pleased with him.)
Source pages p. 635

Commentary
This passage presents rulings on three matters regarding the Haram of Makkah: grazing, cutting plants, and killing insects. The main ruling is that grazing grass in the Haram is generally prohibited, as is cutting any herbage, except for a specific plant called idhkhir (a fragrant grass used for roofing and graves). The justification comes from a hadith where the Prophet exempted idhkhir due to people's needs. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad rule that grazing is prohibited because cutting with animal mouths is analogous to cutting with tools, and since grass can be brought from outside the Haram, there is no necessity. Imam Abu Yusuf, however, permits grazing due to the hardship of repeatedly going in and out of the Haram to bring grass. On killing insects, the text states that killing a louse or locust requires charity (sadaqah), even if a small amount. If the number exceeds three, the obligatory amount is half a sā‘ (approximately 1.5 kg of staple food). This rule is derived from the practice of Caliph Umar. The commentary emphasizes that the act of killing itself is not sinful but requires compensation.

Key Terms
إذخر (idhkhir): A fragrant grass used in the Hijaz for roofing and sealing gaps in graves; exempted from the prohibition of cutting plants in the Haram.
حشيش (hashīsh): Any green grass or herbage in the Haram, generally prohibited from cutting or grazing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on grazing grass in the Haram according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?
A) Permissible without restriction
B) Permissible only for idhkhir
C) Completely prohibited
D) Permissible only in case of necessity

Key Takeaway
The general prohibition on cutting and grazing plants in the Haram is clear; only idhkhir is exempted, and the relied-upon Hanafi view prohibits grazing entirely.

Killing Lice and Locusts in Haram: Charity as Compensation
Ruling on killing lice/locusts in Haram with charity amount

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on killing lice and locusts in the Haram and calculate the required charity (sadaqah).

Core Idea
Killing small pests in the Haram requires charity, not full expiation.

The sanctity of the Haram extends to even the smallest creatures. This lesson explains that killing a louse or locust obligates sadaqah, with specific amounts based on quantity, as derived from the practice of Caliph Umar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The text states that killing a louse or locust in the Haram requires charity (sadaqah). Note that this applies regardless of the number, even if few.
The act is not haram but requires compensation.
2
Recognize the compensation amount
If the number killed exceeds three, the obligatory charity is half a sā‘ (approximately 1.5 kg of staple food). For three or fewer, a small amount (like a handful) suffices.
Reference: 'إذا قتل ما زاد على ثلاثة قملا كان أو جرادا فالواجب نصف صاع'
3
Understand the source of the ruling
The ruling is based on a narration from Caliph Umar (رضي الله عنه), indicating the practice of the early community.
Dalil: qawl sahabi (practice of Umar)
4
Differentiate from other violations
This compensation is sadaqah, not a full expiation (dam) which is required for hunting large game. Lice and locusts are considered minor pests.
Contrast with kaffarah for hunting in ihram.

Prohibition of Cutting and Grazing Haram Vegetation
Ruling on Haram vegetation with proof and intra-madhhab difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on grazing and cutting grass in the Haram, distinguish the exception of idhkhir, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Cutting or grazing Haram grass is prohibited; only idhkhir is exempted by Prophetic permission.

This lesson examines the sacred status of the vegetation of the Haram. While the general rule is that no grass may be cut or grazed, the Prophet ﷺ made an exception for idhkhir due to its essential use in graves and houses. We will also explore Abu Yusuf's dissenting opinion that grazing should be allowed to avoid hardship, and see why the majority Hanafi view remains the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
Read the phrase 'ولا يُرعى الحشيش، ولا يُقطع' and note that both grazing and cutting are prohibited. The passive voice indicates the prohibition applies to all persons, muhrim or not.
The text states: 'ولا يُرعى الحشيش، ولا يُقطع إلا الإذخر'.
2
Understand the exception for idhkhir
Idhkhir is exempted because the Prophet ﷺ permitted it when the Companions noted its need for graves and houses. This exception is specific to idhkhir and does not extend to other grasses.
The hadith cited: 'إنّه لقبورنا وبيوتنا'.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that grazing is prohibited because lips cutting grass is analogous to tools, and grass can be brought from outside. Abu Yusuf permits grazing due to greater need and hardship.
Abu Yusuf's argument: 'الاحتياج إليه فوق الاحتياج إلى الإذخر'.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is the prohibition of grazing (Abu Hanifa's view), as it follows the stronger analogy and the general sanctity of the Haram.
Tarjih authority: the commentary (Sharh al-Wiqayah) adopts this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحج باب الجناية
ولا يُرعى الحشيش، ولا يُقطع إلا الإذخر. وبقتل قملة، أو جرادة صدقة ، وإن قلت.
(ولا يُرعى الحشيش ، ولا يُقطع إلا الإدْخَر) (١) قوله : ولا يُرعى ؛ بصيغة المجهول ؛ هذا عند محمّد وأبي حنيفة ، بناء على أن القطع بالمشافر كالقطع بالمناجل ، وحمل الحشيش من الحل ممكن، فلا ضرورة إلى الرعي بخلاف الإذخر ؛ لأنه استثناه رسولُ الله الله حين قال بعض أصحابه : «إنّه لقبورنا وبيوتنا (۲)، فيجوز قطعه ورعيه. وعند أبي يوسف الله يجوز الرعي ؛ لأنّ الاحتياج إليه فوق الاحتياج إلى الإذخر في خروج الرعاة إلى الحلّ، ثمّ عودهم إليه، أو حمل الحشيش منه كل مرة حرجٌ عظيم. كذا في «البرهان»، وغيره. (٢) قوله : ولا يُقطع ؛ بصيغة المجهول ؛ أي لا يجوز قطع حشيش الحرم لا لمحرم ولا لغيره. [٣] قوله : إلا الإذخر ؛ - بكسر الهمزة ، والخاء المعجمة ، بينهما ذال معجمة ساكنة - : نبت طيب الرائحةِ ببلاد الحجاز، يسقفُ به البيوت بين الخشبات، ويسد به الخلل في القبور بين اللبنات. [٤]قوله : قملة ، بالفتح هو ما يتولّد في ثياب الإنسان وشعره باجتماع الوسخ. [0]قوله : أو جرادة ؛ بالفتح ، بالفارسية : ملخ. : [٦] قوله : صدقة ؛ فإن كثر كما إذا قتل ما زاد على ثلاثة قملاً كان أو جراداً فالواجب نصف صاع. كذا في «البحر»). [٧]قوله : وإن قلت ؛ أي وإن كانت قليلة، ككفّ من طعام، وقد روى عن عمر أنه قال : «تمرة خيرٌ من جرادة» (۲) ، أخرجه مالك، وكذا روي عن ابن عمر عند سعيد بن منصور.
ولا شيء بقتل غراب
(ولا شيء بقتل غراب) [١] قوله : ولا شيء... الخ ؛ الأصل فيه حديث: «يقتلُ المحرم الفارة والغراب والحدأة والعقرب والحيّة والكلب العقور (۳) ، أخرجه البخاري ومسلم. وعند أبي داود : والحية والعقرب والفويسقة والكلب العقور والسبع العادي))) وأما البعوض وما بعده غير السبع الصائل فالوجه في جواز قتلها أنها ليست بصيود، وليست بمتولّدة من البدن والممنوع إنّما هو قتل أحد هذين النوعين.
The Book of Hajj, Chapter of Transgression. And grass is not to be grazed, nor cut except idhkhir. And for killing a louse or locust, charity (is due), even if it is little. And nothing (is due) for killing a crow. Commentary: His saying: 'is not grazed' in passive voice; this is according to Muhammad and Abu Hanifa, based on the principle that cutting with the lips is like cutting with sickles, and carrying grass from the Halal is possible, so there is no need for grazing, contrary to idhkhir because the Messenger of Allah exempted it when some of his Companions said: 'It is for our graves and houses,' so its cutting and grazing are permitted. According to Abu Yusuf, grazing is permitted because the need for it is greater than the need for idhkhir in that shepherds going out to the Halal and then returning, or carrying grass from there each time is a great hardship. Thus in al-Burhan and others. His saying: 'is not cut' in passive voice, meaning it is not permissible to cut the grass of the Haram, neither for a muhrim nor for others. His saying: 'except idhkhir' – with kasra on the hamza, and the mu'jama kha, between them a sakinah dhal – a fragrant plant in the Hijaz, used to roof houses between the beams and to fill gaps in graves between the bricks. His saying: 'louse' is what is generated in people's clothes and hair due to accumulated dirt. His saying: 'or locust' in Persian: malakh. His saying: 'charity' – if numerous, as when one kills more than three, whether lice or locusts, then half a sa' is obligatory. Thus in al-Bahr. His saying: 'even if it is little' meaning even if it is small, like a handful of food, and it is narrated from Umar that he said: 'A date is better than a locust,' transmitted by Malik, and similarly from Ibn Umar via Sa'id ibn Mansur. And nothing (is due) for killing a crow. His saying: 'and nothing...' – the basis for it is the hadith: 'The muhrim kills the mouse, crow, kite, scorpion, snake, and aggressive dog,' reported by Bukhari and Muslim. And according to Abu Dawud: 'and the snake, scorpion, the vermin, the aggressive dog, and the wild beast.' As for mosquitoes and what follows other than the predatory beast, the view on the permissibility of killing them is that they are not game and are not generated from the body; what is prohibited is only killing one of these two types.
Source pages p. 635

Commentary
This passage covers three rulings from the chapter of Hajj penalties. First: the vegetation of the Haram (the sacred precinct) may not be grazed or cut, with the exception of idhkhir grass. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, who analogize grazing by animals to cutting by tools. Since grass can be brought from outside the Haram, there is no necessity to graze inside. Idhkhir is exempted because the Prophet ﷺ specifically allowed it for graves and houses. Abu Yusuf differs, holding that grazing is permissible because the hardship of repeatedly bringing grass from outside is greater. Second: killing a louse or locust in ihram requires sadaqah (charity). The amount depends on the number: for three or fewer, a small amount suffices (e.g., a handful of food); for more than three, half a sa' of grain is obligatory. This is based on the practice of Umar and Ibn Umar. Third: killing a crow incurs no penalty, as it is among the 'five noxious animals' that may be killed in ihram according to the hadith. The commentator explains the rationale: crows are not game and not generated from the body, so killing them is permissible without compensation.

Key Terms
الإذخر (al-idhkhir): A fragrant plant of the Hijaz used for roofing houses and filling gaps in graves.
الحشيش (al-hashish): Wild grass or herbage that grows on the ground.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, what is the ruling on grazing animals in the Haram?
A) Permissible due to need.
B) Prohibited, but idhkhir is an exception.
C) Permissible only for idhkhir.
D) Prohibited only for muhrims.

Key Takeaway
The vegetation of the Haram is sacred; only idhkhir may be cut or grazed, based on the Prophet's permission.

Killing Lice and Locusts in Ihram: Sadaqah Required
Ruling on killing lice and locusts with expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing lice or locusts in ihram, determine the amount of sadaqah depending on the number killed, and identify the evidence from the Companions.

Core Idea
Killing a louse or locust in ihram requires sadaqah; for three or more, half a sa' is due.

While in the state of ihram, most hunting and killing of animals is prohibited. However, small insects like lice and locusts are an exception in that killing them incurs a penalty, but not a full prohibition. The Hanafi school specifies that sadaqah (charity) must be given, with the amount varying based on the quantity killed. This ruling is derived from the practice of the Companions, particularly Umar and Ibn Umar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Killing a louse (qamlah) or locust (jaradah) in ihram requires sadaqah (charity). The text says: 'وبقتل قملة، أو جرادة صدقة' .
Note: The ruling is that sadaqah is wajib (obligatory), not that it is prohibited to kill them.
2
Understand the amount of sadaqah
The commentary explains: if one kills up to three lice or locusts, a small amount like a handful of food suffices. If more than three, half a sa' of grain is obligatory.
The text references al-Bahr for this detail.
3
Identify the evidence
The dalil is the practice (athar) of Umar and Ibn Umar. Umar said: 'A date is better than a locust,' indicating the permissibility of killing with minimal compensation. This is reported by Malik and Sa'id ibn Mansur.
This is a qawl al-sahabi, which is a source of law in the Hanafi school under certain conditions.
4
Distinguish from other animals
Note that lice and locusts are not 'game' (sayd) and are not among the noxious animals that can be killed without penalty. They are creatures that may cause irritation, so the penalty is a light charity.
This contrasts with crows, which have no penalty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحج باب الجناية
ولا يُرعى الحشيش، ولا يُقطع إلا الإذخر. وبقتل قملة، أو جرادة صدقة ، وإن قلت.
(ولا يُرعى الحشيش ، ولا يُقطع إلا الإدْخَر) (١) قوله : ولا يُرعى ؛ بصيغة المجهول ؛ هذا عند محمّد وأبي حنيفة ، بناء على أن القطع بالمشافر كالقطع بالمناجل ، وحمل الحشيش من الحل ممكن، فلا ضرورة إلى الرعي بخلاف الإذخر ؛ لأنه استثناه رسولُ الله الله حين قال بعض أصحابه : «إنّه لقبورنا وبيوتنا (۲)، فيجوز قطعه ورعيه. وعند أبي يوسف الله يجوز الرعي ؛ لأنّ الاحتياج إليه فوق الاحتياج إلى الإذخر في خروج الرعاة إلى الحلّ، ثمّ عودهم إليه، أو حمل الحشيش منه كل مرة حرجٌ عظيم. كذا في «البرهان»، وغيره. (٢) قوله : ولا يُقطع ؛ بصيغة المجهول ؛ أي لا يجوز قطع حشيش الحرم لا لمحرم ولا لغيره. [٣] قوله : إلا الإذخر ؛ - بكسر الهمزة ، والخاء المعجمة ، بينهما ذال معجمة ساكنة - : نبت طيب الرائحةِ ببلاد الحجاز، يسقفُ به البيوت بين الخشبات، ويسد به الخلل في القبور بين اللبنات. [٤]قوله : قملة ، بالفتح هو ما يتولّد في ثياب الإنسان وشعره باجتماع الوسخ. [0]قوله : أو جرادة ؛ بالفتح ، بالفارسية : ملخ. : [٦] قوله : صدقة ؛ فإن كثر كما إذا قتل ما زاد على ثلاثة قملاً كان أو جراداً فالواجب نصف صاع. كذا في «البحر»). [٧]قوله : وإن قلت ؛ أي وإن كانت قليلة، ككفّ من طعام، وقد روى عن عمر أنه قال : «تمرة خيرٌ من جرادة» (۲) ، أخرجه مالك، وكذا روي عن ابن عمر عند سعيد بن منصور.
ولا شيء بقتل غراب
(ولا شيء بقتل غراب) [١] قوله : ولا شيء... الخ ؛ الأصل فيه حديث: «يقتلُ المحرم الفارة والغراب والحدأة والعقرب والحيّة والكلب العقور (۳) ، أخرجه البخاري ومسلم. وعند أبي داود : والحية والعقرب والفويسقة والكلب العقور والسبع العادي))) وأما البعوض وما بعده غير السبع الصائل فالوجه في جواز قتلها أنها ليست بصيود، وليست بمتولّدة من البدن والممنوع إنّما هو قتل أحد هذين النوعين.
The Book of Hajj, Chapter of Transgression. And grass is not to be grazed, nor cut except idhkhir. And for killing a louse or locust, charity (is due), even if it is little. And nothing (is due) for killing a crow. Commentary: His saying: 'is not grazed' in passive voice; this is according to Muhammad and Abu Hanifa, based on the principle that cutting with the lips is like cutting with sickles, and carrying grass from the Halal is possible, so there is no need for grazing, contrary to idhkhir because the Messenger of Allah exempted it when some of his Companions said: 'It is for our graves and houses,' so its cutting and grazing are permitted. According to Abu Yusuf, grazing is permitted because the need for it is greater than the need for idhkhir in that shepherds going out to the Halal and then returning, or carrying grass from there each time is a great hardship. Thus in al-Burhan and others. His saying: 'is not cut' in passive voice, meaning it is not permissible to cut the grass of the Haram, neither for a muhrim nor for others. His saying: 'except idhkhir' – with kasra on the hamza, and the mu'jama kha, between them a sakinah dhal – a fragrant plant in the Hijaz, used to roof houses between the beams and to fill gaps in graves between the bricks. His saying: 'louse' is what is generated in people's clothes and hair due to accumulated dirt. His saying: 'or locust' in Persian: malakh. His saying: 'charity' – if numerous, as when one kills more than three, whether lice or locusts, then half a sa' is obligatory. Thus in al-Bahr. His saying: 'even if it is little' meaning even if it is small, like a handful of food, and it is narrated from Umar that he said: 'A date is better than a locust,' transmitted by Malik, and similarly from Ibn Umar via Sa'id ibn Mansur. And nothing (is due) for killing a crow. His saying: 'and nothing...' – the basis for it is the hadith: 'The muhrim kills the mouse, crow, kite, scorpion, snake, and aggressive dog,' reported by Bukhari and Muslim. And according to Abu Dawud: 'and the snake, scorpion, the vermin, the aggressive dog, and the wild beast.' As for mosquitoes and what follows other than the predatory beast, the view on the permissibility of killing them is that they are not game and are not generated from the body; what is prohibited is only killing one of these two types.
Source pages p. 635

Commentary
This passage covers three rulings from the chapter of Hajj penalties. First: the vegetation of the Haram (the sacred precinct) may not be grazed or cut, with the exception of idhkhir grass. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, who analogize grazing by animals to cutting by tools. Since grass can be brought from outside the Haram, there is no necessity to graze inside. Idhkhir is exempted because the Prophet ﷺ specifically allowed it for graves and houses. Abu Yusuf differs, holding that grazing is permissible because the hardship of repeatedly bringing grass from outside is greater. Second: killing a louse or locust in ihram requires sadaqah (charity). The amount depends on the number: for three or fewer, a small amount suffices (e.g., a handful of food); for more than three, half a sa' of grain is obligatory. This is based on the practice of Umar and Ibn Umar. Third: killing a crow incurs no penalty, as it is among the 'five noxious animals' that may be killed in ihram according to the hadith. The commentator explains the rationale: crows are not game and not generated from the body, so killing them is permissible without compensation.

Key Terms
القملة (al-qamlah): A louse that breeds in human clothes and hair due to accumulated dirt.
الجرادة (al-jaradah): Locust, a type of grasshopper.
صدقة (sadaqah): Charity; here it means a specific expiatory charity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a muhrim kills two lice and one locust, what sadaqah is due?
A) Half a sa' because total is three.
B) A handful of food for each, i.e., three handfuls.
C) A handful of food total, because total does not exceed three distinct insects.
D) No sadaqah because locusts are different from lice.

Key Takeaway
Lice and locusts killed in ihram require sadaqah: a handful for up to three, half a sa' for more.

Killing Crows in Ihram: No Penalty
Ruling on killing crows with hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing crows in ihram, cite the hadith of the five noxious animals, and explain why no compensation is required.

Core Idea
Crows are among the noxious animals that may be killed in ihram without any penalty.

The Prophet ﷺ listed five types of animals that a muhrim may kill without incurring any penalty. One of these is the crow. This lesson examines the hadith evidence and the rationale behind this exception, contrasting it with other creatures like lice and locusts which require sadaqah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The text states: 'ولا شيء بقتل غراب' meaning nothing (no penalty) is due for killing a crow.
This is the summary ruling.
2
Examine the hadith evidence
The hadith of the five noxious animals: 'يقتل المحرم الفارة والغراب والحدأة والعقرب والحية والكلب العقور' . This is the primary dalil.
The hadith is narrated by Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Understand the rationale
The commentator explains that crows and similar animals are not game (not hunted for food) and are not generated from the human body. The prohibition of killing in ihram is limited to those two categories (hunting and insects from body).
Thus, crows fall outside the prohibited categories.
4
Distinguish from other rulings
Contrast with lice/locusts (require sadaqah) and other animals that are not exempted. The list is exhaustive.
The hadith grants blanket permission for these five.
