Ruling on Killing a Crow During Ihram
Ruling and proof for killing a crow in ihram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing a crow during ihram and identify the proof from hadith.

Core Idea
A pilgrim may kill harmful animals like the crow without penalty; only game and body-generated pests require compensation.

This lesson examines a specific exemption in ihram: killing the crow. The Hanafi school, based on a sound hadith, rules that no fidyah is owed for killing crows and similar harmful creatures. We will explore the proof and the underlying legal reasoning (wajh).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The matn states: 'ولا شيء بقتل غراب' meaning there is no penalty for killing a crow. This is the core ruling for a pilgrim in ihram.
The term 'غراب' here refers specifically to the pied crow that eats carrion.
2
Locate the Dalil
The author cites the hadith: 'يقتل المحرم الفارة والغراب والحدأة والعقرب والحية والكلب العقور' (The pilgrim may kill the mouse, crow, kite, scorpion, snake, and vicious dog). This is from Bukhari and Muslim.
The imperative 'يقتل' indicates permissibility (ibahah), especially since it is exempting from penalty.
3
Analyze the Wajh al-Dalalah
The hadith lists harmful animals that are not considered game (sayd). The crow is included because it is a pest, not a game animal. Thus, killing it does not trigger the prohibition on hunting.
Hanafis differentiate between sayd (edible, wild animals) and harmful creatures; only the former incur fidyah.
4
Extend the Principle
The commentary explains that other harmful animals like mosquitoes also fall under this exemption, provided they are not generated from the pilgrim's body (like lice). The reasoning is that they are not sayd and not body-generated pests.
The two prohibited categories for killing in ihram are: (1) sayd, and (2) pests originating from the body (like lice).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا شيء بقتل غراب. الأصل فيه حديث: «يقتل المحرم الفارة والغراب والحدأة والعقرب والحية والكلب العقور» أخرجه البخاري ومسلم. وعند أبي داود: والحية والعقرب والفويسقة والكلب العقور والسبع العادي. وأما البعوض وما بعده غير السبع الصائل فالوجه في جواز قتلها أنها ليست بصيود، وليست بمتولدة من البدن والممنوع إنما هو قتل أحد هذين النوعين.
And there is nothing [due] for killing a crow. The basis for this is the hadith: 'The pilgrim may kill the mouse, the crow, the kite, the scorpion, the snake, and the vicious dog' narrated by Bukhari and Muslim. And according to Abu Dawud: '...the snake, the scorpion, the harmful [animal], the vicious dog, and the predatory beast.' As for mosquitoes and what follows them other than the predatory beast, the reasoning for permissibility of killing them is that they are not game and not generated from the body; rather, the prohibition is only for killing one of these two types.
Source pages p. 636

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that a pilgrim in ihram may kill a crow without incurring any penalty (dam or fidyah). The author then provides the proof: a hadith from Bukhari and Muslim listing five harmful animals that may be killed in ihram, including the crow. Abu Dawud's version adds 'predatory beast.' The commentary then explains the legal reasoning (wajh) for allowing the killing of other harmful creatures like mosquitoes: they are neither game (which is ordinarily forbidden to a pilgrim) nor are they generated from the pilgrim's body (like lice, which require fidyah). Thus, the prohibition of killing is restricted to those two categories, and anything else that causes harm may be killed without liability.

Key Terms
غراب (ghuraab): The crow, specifically the pied crow that eats carrion (as opposed to agricultural crows).
صيد (sayd): Game animals that are lawful to hunt; killing them during ihram incurs a penalty.
فارة (faarah): Mouse or rat; listed among harmful animals that can be killed in ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited in this lesson, which of the following animals is a pilgrim allowed to kill?
A) Camel
B) Crow
C) Gazelle
D) Sheep

Key Takeaway
Killing harmful animals like the crow during ihram does not incur any penalty; the prohibition is only on killing game.

Animals Permissible to Kill in Ihram: Lexical Definitions
Lexical definitions of permissible animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the specific animals a pilgrim in ihram may kill based on their lexical definitions and distinguish them from game animals.

Core Idea
Killing harmful creatures in ihram is permissible; the specific animals are defined by their harmful nature, not by species alone.

This passage provides lexical definitions for the animals listed in the previous ruling on permissible killing in ihram. Understanding these definitions is essential for correctly applying the permission and avoiding confusion with game animals that require fidyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the listed animals
Read through the Arabic list: kite, scorpion, snake, mouse, vicious dog, mosquito, flea, tick, and turtle. Note each term.
These are additional to the crow mentioned earlier.
2
Analyze the lexical definitions
The commentator defines each animal, often with Persian equivalents. For example, 'kite' is a predatory bird; 'vicious dog' includes any predatory animal that attacks and wounds.
Focus on the defining characteristics: harmfulness, not mere species.
3
Understand the operative principle
The unifying factor is that these creatures are harmful (e.g., poison, predation, disease). This justifies the permission to kill them in ihram without penalty.
The Hanafi school relies on the general permission from hadith for harmful animals.
4
Differentiate from similar animals
Consider animals not listed: e.g., a dove is not harmful, so killing it would require fidyah. The definitions help make this distinction.
The turtle is included; some other schools consider it game – note the ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحدأة، وعقرب، وحية، وفارة، وكلب عقور، وبعوض، وبرغوث، وقُراد، وسلحفاة. قوله: وحدأة؛ بكسر الحاء المهملة وفتح الدال المهملة، بعدها همزة مفتوحة، وحكى المد أيضاً بالفارسية: زغن. قوله: وعقرب؛ بفتح العين والراء، بينهما قاف ساكنة: كثر دم. قوله: وحية؛ بفتح الحاء المهملة وتشديد الياء، بالفارسية: مار. قوله: عقور؛ بالفتح: هو الذي يعقر ويؤذي. قوله: وبرغوث؛ بضم الأول والثالث، وسكون الثاني: كيك. قوله: وسلحفاة؛ بضم السين، وفتح اللام وسكون الحاء، باخه بالهندية: كچھوا.
And a kite, and a scorpion, and a snake, and a mouse, and a vicious dog, and mosquitoes, and fleas, and ticks, and a turtle. He said (the commentator): 'And a kite' – with a kasra on the ha and fatha on the dal, followed by a hamza with fatha; also pronounced with madd; in Persian: zaghan. 'And a scorpion' – with fatha on the ayn and ra, with a sukun on the qaf between them: much poison. 'And a snake' – with fatha on the ha and tashdid on the ya; in Persian: mar. 'Vicious' – with fatha: one that attacks and harms. 'And a flea' – with dhamma on the first and third letters, sukun on the second: kik. 'And a turtle' – with dhamma on the seen, fatha on the lam, and sukun on the ha; in Hindi: kachhua.
Source pages p. 637

Commentary
This passage lists additional animals that a pilgrim in ihram may kill without penalty, building on the previous ruling about the crow. The commentator then provides lexical definitions for each term to clarify exactly which creatures are meant. The kite (حدأة) is a bird of prey, the scorpion (عقرب) is poisonous, the snake (حية) is a serpent, the vicious dog (كلب عقور) is any predatory animal that attacks (like a lion, leopard, wolf), the flea (برغوث) is a small jumping insect, the tick (قراد) is a type of mite that sucks blood, and the turtle (سلحفاة) is a reptile. These definitions help ensure that the ruling applies only to these specific harmful creatures and not to similar ones that might be considered game or body parasites.

Key Terms
حدأة (hidā'ah): A kite; a predatory bird that preys on rats and poultry.
كلب عقور (kalb 'aqūr): A vicious dog; any predatory animal that attacks and wounds, including lions, leopards, wolves, and dogs.
سلحفاة (sulḥafāh): Turtle or tortoise; a reptile that is not considered game and may be killed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the meaning of 'كلب عقور' (vicious dog)?
A) Only a dog that bites
B) Any predatory animal that attacks and wounds
C) A rabies-infected dog
D) All canines

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits killing specific harmful creatures in ihram without fidyah; lexical definitions ensure correct identification.

Rulings on Animals and Game for a Muhrim
Lists permissible animals to kill, slaughter, and rules on game

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories of rulings regarding animals and game for a Muhrim: permissible killing of harmful animals, permissible slaughter of domestic animals and eating game from a non-Muhrim without participation, and the obligation to release game in the Haram and rescind its sale.

Core Idea
A Muhrim may kill harmful animals, slaughter domestic ones, and eat game from a non-Muhrim—but must release any game in the Haram.

This passage from the chapter on Hajj penalties (bāb al-jināya) clarifies three essential rulings that every pilgrim should know. Understanding when killing is allowed, when slaughter is permissible, and the special sanctity of the Haram prevents inadvertent violations. The commentary expands each ruling with proofs and conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the list of permissible killings
Read the list of animals a Muhrim may kill without penalty: kite, scorpion, snake, mouse, predatory dog, mosquito, flea, tick, turtle, and any predatory beast that attacks. Note that this is an exception to the general prohibition of hunting in iḥrām.
The list is exhaustive in the Ḥanafī school; other animals require compensation if killed.
2
Understand the condition for predatory beasts
For predatory beasts (sabuʿ ṣā’il), killing is only allowed if the beast attacks. The commentator emphasises that the Muhrim is prohibited from harming creatures, but not from repelling harm.
This is a Ḥanafī condition; Shāfiʿīs allow killing all predatory animals regardless.
3
Learn the ruling on slaughtering domestic animals
The Muhrim may slaughter domestic animals like sheep, cow, camel, chicken, and domestic duck. These are not considered ṣayd (game) because they are not wild.
Domestic ducks are those raised in homes and ponds.
4
Learn the conditions for eating game from a non-Muhrim
A Muhrim may eat game hunted and slaughtered by a non-Muhrim, provided the Muhrim did not participate through dalāla (pointing), ishāra (signaling), or amr (ordering). The proof is the ḥadīth of Abū Qatāda.
Dalāla includes any indication; the Ḥanafī school treats it like direct hunting.
5
Understand the obligation when entering the Haram with game
If a Muhrim enters the sanctuary (ḥaram) with live game, he must release it. If he already sold it, the sale must be rescinded if the animal is still present. This is due to the sanctity of the Haram.
The release must be in a way that does not harm the animal.

Source Text

Chapter of Hajj, Section of Penalties. Kite, scorpion, snake, mouse, predatory dog, mosquito, flea, tick, turtle, and predatory beast. He may slaughter sheep, cow, camel, chicken, domestic duck, and eat what a non-Muhrim hunted and slaughtered without the Muhrim's indication or command. Whoever enters the Haram with game must release it and rescind its sale if it remains.
Source pages p. 637

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on Hajj penalties (bāb al-jināya) addresses three rulings for a person in iḥrām. First, the Muhrim may kill certain harmful animals without incurring any penalty (jazā’). These include predatory birds, scorpions, snakes, mice, dogs that attack, mosquitoes, fleas, ticks, turtles, and any beast that assaults humans. The commentator explains that the list is limited to creatures that cause harm or are inherently filthy; otherwise, killing other animals would require compensation. Second, the Muhrim may slaughter domestic animals like sheep, cows, camels, chickens, and domestic ducks—these are not considered ṣayd (game) because they are not wild. He may also eat meat from game hunted and slaughtered by a non-Muhrim, provided the Muhrim did not assist by pointing, signaling, or ordering the hunt. The proof is the hadith of Abū Qatāda, where the Prophet permitted a Muhrim to eat game caught by a non-Muhrim. Third, if a Muhrim enters the sanctuary (ḥaram) with game in his possession, he must release it; if he already sold it and the animal is still within the sanctuary, the sale must be rescinded because of the sanctity of the Haram. The commentator notes that the release must be in a way that does not harm the animal, and the seller is obligated to cancel the transaction.

Key Terms
سبع صائل (sabuʿ ṣā’il): A predatory beast that attacks or assails a person, such as a wolf or lion; killing it is permissible for a Muhrim if it is attacking.
صيد (ṣayd): Wild game that is hunted; domestic animals are not ṣayd.
دلالة (dalāla): Indication or pointing; if a Muhrim points at game, that constitutes participation and forbids eating it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What should a Muhrim do if he enters the Haram with live game in his possession?
A) He may keep it and sell it.
B) He must release it and rescind any sale if the animal remains.
C) He must slaughter it immediately.
D) He may keep it but cannot eat it.

Key Takeaway
A Muhrim has clear permissions for harmful animals, domestic slaughter, and game from others, but must release any game brought into the Haram.

Rulings for a Muhrim: Predatory Animals and Domestic Slaughter
Rulings on killing predatory animals and slaughtering domestic animals for a muhrim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on when a muhrim may kill a predatory animal, slaughter domestic animals, and eat game from a non-muhrim, and identify the condition that permits the latter.

Core Idea
A muhrim may kill an attacking predator and slaughter domestic animals; but may eat game only if he did not assist in the hunt.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies three common questions for someone in ihram. The general prohibition against hunting during hajj has specific exceptions that every muhrim must know. We will learn exactly when a predatory animal may be killed, which animals are permissible to slaughter for food, and the vital condition for eating game obtained by others.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three separate rulings
The passage presents three distinct permissions: killing a predatory animal, slaughtering domestic animals, and eating game from a non-muhrim. Isolate each one.
2
Examine the predatory animal ruling
The text limits killing to a 'sabu' sa'il'—a predatory animal that attacks. If it does not attack, the muhrim cannot kill it because the prohibition is on aggression, not on repelling harm.
The key distinction: defense vs. offense.
3
Examine the domestic slaughter ruling
Domestic animals like sheep, cows, camels, chickens, and domestic ducks are not considered game because they are tame (alf bi-asl al-khilqah). The muhrim may slaughter and eat them normally.
Wild ducks would be game and require fidyah; domestic ducks are excluded.
4
Analyze the condition for eating game from a non-muhrim
The muhrim may eat game hunted by a non-muhrim only if the muhrim did not assist by guidance (dalalah), indication (isharah), or command (amr). This ruling is based on the hadith of Abu Qatada.
If the muhrim even points to the animal, it becomes impermissible for him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسبع صائل (۱۷۱) . (۲) وله ذبح الشاة ، والبقر والبعير، والدجاج، والبط الأهلي ) ، وأكل ما 6 صادَهُ حلال وذبحه بلا دلالة محرم، وأمره به .
[١]قوله : وسبع صائل ؛ الذي يحملُ على الإنسان للإيذاء كالذئب والأسد، فإن لم يؤذ فلا يقتله، فإن المحرم ممنوع عن التعرض لا عن دفع الأذى.
[٢]قوله : ذبح الشاة ... الخ ؛ فإنّ هذه الأشياء ليست بصيود، ولعدم التوحش. والبط الأهلي ؛ هو الذي يكون في المساكن والحياض ؛ لأنه ألوف بأصل الخلقة.
[٣]قوله : وأكل ... الخ ؛ أي يجوز للمحرم أكل ما صاده غير محرم وذبحه من غير أن يشترك فيه المحرم دلالة وإشارة، أو أمر به، يؤخذ ذلك من حديث أبي قتادة له ، وقد مر ذكره.
[ ] قوله : وذبحه ؛ أي ذلك الحلال ؛ فإن ذبحه المحرم فلا يجوز، فإن ذبيحته ميتة.
And a predatory animal (that attacks). And he may slaughter sheep, cows, camels, chickens, and domestic ducks, and eat what a non-muhrim hunted and slaughtered without the muhrim's guidance, indication, or command.
[1] His saying: 'and a predatory animal' – that which attacks humans, like a wolf and lion; if it does not attack, he should not kill it, for the muhrim is prevented from aggression, not from repelling harm.
[2] His saying: 'slaughter the sheep...' etc., because these things are not game and are not wild. Domestic ducks are those in houses and water tanks because they are tame by nature.
[3] His saying: 'and eat...' etc., meaning it is permissible for the muhrim to eat what a non-muhrim hunted and slaughtered without the muhrim participating by guidance, indication, or command. This is taken from the hadith of Abu Qatada, which has been mentioned.
[ ] His saying: 'and his slaughter' – meaning that of the non-muhrim; if the muhrim slaughtered it, it is not permissible, because his slaughter is carrion.
Source pages p. 638

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Sunnah. It addresses three issues for a person in ihram (muhrim). First, a muhrim may kill a predatory animal like a wolf or lion if it attacks him, because the prohibition in ihram is against hunting, not against repelling harm. If the animal does not attack, he may not kill it. Second, a muhrim may slaughter domestic animals such as sheep, cows, camels, chickens, and domestic ducks, because they are not game and are tame by nature. Third, a muhrim may eat game that a non-muhrim (halal) hunted and slaughtered, provided the muhrim did not assist by guiding, pointing, or commanding. This is established by the hadith of Abu Qatada, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) permitted eating game brought by a non-muhrim. If the muhrim himself slaughters the game, it is not permissible because his slaughter is invalid (carrion).

Key Terms
سبع صائل (sabuʿ ṣāʾil): A predatory animal that attacks humans aggressively, like a wolf or lion.
بط أهلي (baṭṭ ahli): Domestic ducks that live in houses and water tanks, tame by nature, not considered game.
دلالة (dalālah): Guidance or indication by the muhrim that assists in hunting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following actions is permissible for a muhrim according to the Hanafi school?
A) Killing a sleeping lion.
B) Slaughtering a domestic duck for food.
C) Eating game hunted by a non-muhrim who was guided to the animal by the muhrim's pointing.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
A muhrim may take animal life only in self-defense (predators that attack) or for nourishment (domestic animals), but may never actively participate in hunting or benefit from game he assisted in catching.

Entering the Haram with Game: Release, Sale, and Compensation
Ruling on entering Haram with game and sale by muhrim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligations of a muhrim who enters the Haram with a hunted animal and apply the exception for game already owned before ihram.

Core Idea
Entering the Haram with game requires release; selling it requires annulment; if it perishes, compensation is wajib.

This passage addresses a specific scenario: a pilgrim who enters the sacred precinct of Makkah (Haram) while carrying a hunted animal. The sanctity of the Haram grants protection to all game, so the pilgrim must release it. If he sold the animal inside the Haram, the sale is invalid and must be annulled if the animal still exists; otherwise, he must pay compensation. However, an important exception exists: if the animal was already in his house or cage when he assumed ihram, he may keep it because ownership is not negated by ihram. The proof comes from the actions of the Companions, particularly Ali and Abdullah ibn al-Harith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling
Locate the first sentence: whoever enters the Haram with a hunted animal must release it. This is the default obligation.
2
Analyze the sale scenario
If the muhrim sold the animal after entering, the sale must be annulled if the animal remains. If it perished, he must pay compensation (jaza). This applies whether the sale was to a muhrim or non-muhrim, and inside or outside the Haram.
3
Understand the exception
If the animal was in his house or in a cage with him at the time of assuming ihram, he is not required to release it. The commentary cites narrations from Ali and Ibn al-Harith as proof.
4
Examine the discussion on 'with him'
The commentary debates whether 'cage with him' means in hand or in luggage. The stronger view is that the bird itself is not in hand, so release is not obligatory, analogous to carrying a mushaf in its cover.
5
Derive the underlying principle
The rule distinguishes between game that becomes 'sayd al-haram' (game of the Haram) by entry and game already owned before ihram. Ownership rights continue despite ihram, but entering the Haram transforms the status of the animal.

Source Text

And whoever enters the Haram with a hunted animal must release it and annul its sale if it remains.
[0] His saying: 'release it', meaning it is obligatory upon him to release it if it is in his hand, due to the sanctity of the Haram, and to annul the sale and cancel it if the hunted animal he sold after entering the Haram is still extant.
... (footnote on سبع صائل) ...
Otherwise, he must compensate, like the sale of a muhrim's hunted animal: i.e., annul the sale if it remains, otherwise compensate, whether he sold it to a muhrim or a non-muhrim.
Not a hunted animal in his house or in a cage with him when he assumes ihram: i.e., if he assumes ihram while a hunted animal is in his house or in his cage, he is not obliged to release it, because ihram does not negate ownership of the hunted animal and its care, unlike the one who enters the Haram with a hunted animal, for that hunted animal becomes a hunted animal of the Haram, so he must refrain from harming it.
[1] His saying: 'his entry'; said in al-Fath: whether the sale was in the Haram or after taking it out to the hill, because by bringing it in it became a hunted animal of the Haram, so taking it out is not permissible.
[2] His saying: 'otherwise compensate'; meaning if it is not extant but perished in the buyer's hand, he must compensate because he harmed the hunted animal by depriving it of the security it deserved.
[3] His saying: 'like the sale of a muhrim's hunted animal'; for if a muhrim sells a hunted animal he hunted, he must annul the sale if it remains, and compensate if it perishes.
[4] His saying: 'not a hunted animal in his house'; indeed, Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) saw a tame hunted animal with his companions while they were in ihram, and he did not order them to release it, reported by Ibn Abi Shayba, and also reported from Abdullah ibn al-Harith who said: 'We used to perform Hajj and leave with our families things of hunted animals that we did not release.'
[0] His saying: 'or in a cage'; with two fathas: a cage for birds.
[6] His saying: 'with him'; said in al-Nihaya: it is possible he meant by 'with him' that it is in his hand, and possible he meant in his luggage or with his servant. One might say: if it is with him in his hand, he should release it because the cage being with him means it is in his hand. Another might say: the bird is not in his hand, even if the cage is in his hand, so he is not obliged to release, just as a junub carrying a mushaf in its cover is not disliked and is not like taking the mushaf itself. So mentioned by Faqih Abu Ja'far.
Source pages p. 638

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the actions of the Companions. It addresses what a person must do if he enters the Haram (sanctuary of Makkah) while carrying a hunted animal. The ruling states that he must release the animal because the Haram grants security to game. If he sold the animal after entering the Haram, he must annul the sale if the animal is still in the buyer's possession; if it perished, he must pay compensation (jaza). This applies whether he sold it to a muhrim or a non-muhrim, and whether the sale occurred inside or outside the Haram. The exception is if the animal was in his house or in a cage with him when he assumed ihram; in that case, he does not have to release it because ihram does not invalidate ownership of game already in one's possession. The commentary cites narrations from Ali and Abdullah ibn al-Harith showing that Companions did not release game they had before ihram. The discussion also clarifies that if the cage is in his hand but the bird is inside, it is not considered 'in his hand' for release purposes, analogizing to carrying a mushaf in a cover.

Key Terms
حرم (ḥaram): The sacred precincts of Makkah where hunting and harming game is prohibited.
رد البيع (radd al-bayʿ): Annulment of a sale, here required if the hunted animal sold in the Haram still exists.
جزاء (jazāʾ): Compensation (usually a sacrifice) for harming game in ihram or in the Haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a muhrim sells a hunted animal he brought into the Haram and the animal perishes in the buyer's hands, what must he do?
A) Nothing, because the sale is valid.
B) Annul the sale and return the price.
C) Pay compensation (jaza).
D) Both annul the sale and pay compensation.

Key Takeaway
Entering the Haram with game obligates its release or compensation if sold, but game owned before ihram in one's house or cage is exempt.

Ruling on a Muhrim Selling Game and the Exception for Domestic Game
Commentary expanding the ruling on Muhrim selling game with exception for domestic game

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a Muhrim selling game, identify the exception for game in his house or cage, and explain the legal reasoning behind the exception.

Core Idea
A Muhrim's sale of hunted game is void; but game already in his home or cage remains his property.

The prohibition on hunting while in ihram raises questions about what a Muhrim may do with game. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies that if a Muhrim sells game he hunted, the sale is invalid. However, an important exception exists: game that was already in his house or cage before entering ihram is not affected, because ihram does not negate ownership. Understanding this distinction helps apply the rules correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a Muhrim selling game he hunted. The ruling is that the sale is void (batil). If the game is still in the buyer's possession, the sale must be revoked. If the game has perished, the Muhrim must pay compensation.
This applies whether the buyer is a Muhrim or not.
2
Recognize the exception
The text states an exception: game that is in the Muhrim's house or in a cage with him at the time he enters ihram is not subject to this ruling. He is not required to release it.
The exception is based on the principle that ihram does not nullify ownership of game acquired before ihram.
3
Understand the rationale
Ihram restricts hunting and handling of game, but it does not void existing ownership. Therefore, game already in one's possession before ihram remains lawful to keep. This is unlike game brought into the Haram, which becomes protected by the sanctity of the Haram and must be released.
The analogy with carrying a Mushaf in its cover while in janaba illustrates that the bird in a cage is not considered 'in hand' for legal purposes.
4
Apply the exception to scenarios
The exception covers game in the house, in a cage, or even with a servant. The key is that the Muhrim had lawful possession of the game before ihram.
If the cage is in his hand, the bird is not considered in his hand; therefore release is not required.
5
Distinguish from entering the Haram with game
If a person enters the Haram with game (e.g., after hunting outside), the game becomes protected by the Haram's sanctity. He must then avoid harming it, and his ownership is affected. This is different from the case of a Muhrim who already had game at home before ihram.
The passage emphasizes this distinction using the footnote from al-Fath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلا جزى كبيع المحرم صيده، لا صيدا في بيته، أو في قفص معه إن أحرم أي رد البيع الذي أتى به في إحرامه بعد دخوله في الحرم إن بقي الصيد في يد المشتري، وإلا جزى كبيع المحرم صيده: أي ردَّ بيعه إن بقي، وإلا جزى سواء باعه من محرم أو حلال لا صيدا في بيته، أو في قفص معه إن أحرم أي إن أحرم وفي بيته أو قفصه صيد ليس عليه أن يرسله، لأن الإحرام لا يُنافي مالكيةَ الصَّيدِ ومحافظته، بخلاف مَن دَخَلَ الحرم بصيد، فإنَّ الصَّيدَ صارَ صيد الحرم، فيجب ترك التعرض له [١]قوله: دخوله؛ قال في «الفتح»: سواءً كان بيعه في الحرم أو بعدما أخرجه إلى الحل؛ لأنه صار بالإدخال من صيد الحرم، فلا يحلّ إخراجه. [٢] قوله: وإلا جزى؛ أي وإن لم يكن باقياً، بل هلك في يد المشتري فعلـيـه الجزاء؛ لأنه تعرض للصيد بتفويت الأمن الذي استحقه. [٣] قوله: كبيع المحرم صيده؛ فإنّه إذا باع المحرم الصيد الذي صاده وجب عليه ردّ بيعه إن كان باقياً، والجزاء إن كان هالكاً. [٤] قوله: لا صيداً في بيته؛ فإنّ عليا الله رأى مع أصحابه داجناً من الصيد وهم محرمون، فلم يأمرهم بإرساله، أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة، وأخرج أيضاً عن عبد الله بن الحارث قال: كنا نحج ونترك عند أهلنا أشياء من الصيد ما نرسلها. [٥] قوله: أو في قفص؛ بفتحتين: ينجره مرغان. [٦] قوله: معه؛ قال في «النهاية»: يحتمل أنه أراد من قوله: «معه» أنه في يده، ويحتمل أنه أراد في رحله، أو مع خادمه وكان لقائل أن يقول: إذا كان معه في يده ينبغي أن يرسله؛ لأنَّ القفص متى كان معه كان في يده، ولقائل أن يقول: ليس الطير في يده، وإن كان القفص في يده فلا يلزمُهُ الإرسال، فإنّ الجنب إذا حمل مصحفاً في غلافه لم يكره، ولم يكن ذلك كأخذ المصحف، كذا ذكره الفقيه أبو جعفر.
Otherwise he must pay compensation, like the sale of game by a Muhrim. Not game in his house, or in a cage with him when he enters ihram. That is, he revokes the sale which he transacted during his ihram after entering the Haram, if the game remains in the buyer's hand; otherwise he pays compensation, like the sale of game by a Muhrim: he must revoke the sale if it remains, otherwise he pays compensation, whether he sold it to a Muhrim or a non-Muhrim. Not game in his house, or in a cage with him when he enters ihram: i.e., if he enters ihram and in his house or cage there is game, he is not required to release it, because ihram does not negate ownership of game and its preservation, unlike the one who enters the Haram with game, for then the game becomes game of the Haram, and it becomes obligatory to avoid harming it. [1] His saying 'his entering'; it is said in al-Fath: whether his sale was in the Haram or after he took it out to the non-Haram, because by bringing it in it became game of the Haram, so its removal is not permissible. [2] His saying 'otherwise he pays compensation'; i.e., if it is not remaining but perishes in the buyer's hand, then upon him is the compensation, because he interfered with the game by depriving it of the security it deserved. [3] His saying 'like the sale of game by a Muhrim'; for when a Muhrim sells game he has hunted, he must revoke the sale if it remains, and pay compensation if it is destroyed. [4] His saying 'not game in his house'; indeed Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) saw with his companions a domesticated game bird while they were in ihram, and he did not order them to release it, narrated by Ibn Abi Shayba, and also narrated from Abdullah ibn al-Harith that he said: we used to perform Hajj and leave with our families some game things without releasing them. [5] His saying 'or in a cage'; with two fathas: yanjuruhu muraghan. [6] His saying 'with him'; it is said in al-Nihaya: it is possible that by 'with him' he meant in his hand, and it is possible he meant in his baggage or with his servant. And one might say: if it is with him in his hand, he should release it, because the cage when with him is in his hand; and another might say: the bird is not in his hand, even if the cage is in his hand, so he is not obliged to release it, just as a person in janaba if he carries a Mushaf in its cover is not disliked, and that is not like taking the Mushaf; thus mentioned by Faqih Abu Ja'far.
Source pages p. 639

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expansion of a ruling from the matn of al-Wiqaya. It addresses the situation where a Muhrim (a person in the state of ihram) sells game (صيد). The ruling is that the sale is void (رد) if the game is still in the buyer's possession; otherwise, the Muhrim must pay compensation (جزاء). This applies whether the buyer is also a Muhrim or not. The key exception is for game that was in the Muhrim's house or in a cage with him at the time he entered ihram. In that case, he is not required to release it, because ihram does not nullify ownership of game. The commentator explains that this is unlike the case where a person enters the Haram (the sacred precinct) with game, because then the game becomes protected by the sanctity of the Haram and must be released. The commentary also clarifies that 'with him' in the phrase 'or in a cage with him' can mean in his hand, in his baggage, or with his servant. It gives a helpful analogy: just as a person in a state of major impurity (janaba) can carry a Mushaf in its cover without it being considered as taking the Mushaf directly, similarly, the bird in the cage is not considered to be in the Muhrim's hand, so he is not obligated to release it. The passage also cites a narration from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) where he saw domesticated game with his companions in ihram and did not order them to release it, establishing the principle.

Key Terms
صيد (sayd): Game, i.e., wild animals or birds that are lawful to hunt.
محرم (muhrim): A person who has entered the state of ihram for Hajj or Umrah.
قفص (qafas): A cage for confining birds or animals.
جزاء (jaza'): Compensation, often a penalty for violating ihram restrictions on game.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a Muhrim sells game that he hunted during ihram and the game is still alive in the buyer's hands?
A) The sale is valid and the Muhrim keeps the profit.
B) The sale is void and the Muhrim must revoke it, returning the price.
C) The sale is void but the Muhrim must pay compensation.
D) The sale is valid unless the buyer is also a Muhrim.

Key Takeaway
A Muhrim's sale of game hunted during ihram is void; but game owned before ihram (e.g., in house or cage) is not subject to this restriction.

Releasing Game from a Muhrim's Hand: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for releasing game from a Muhrim’s hand.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on liability for releasing game from a Muhrim's hand, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Ownership before ihram is respected; releasing game is destruction, not commanding good.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: if a non-Muhrim captures game, then enters ihram, and someone else releases that game from his hand, is the releaser liable? Imam Abu Hanifa holds the releaser liable for compensation, while his two leading students disagree. The key lies in whether the Muhrim's ownership of the game is respected or nullified by ihram.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the dispute
The scenario: a non-Muhrim captures game, then enters ihram. Another person releases the game from his hand. The dispute: Is the releaser liable for compensation?
The game was lawfully owned before ihram.
2
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa's view)
Abu Hanifa: The releaser is liable because the owner had valid ownership (milk muhtaram) at the time of release, and ihram does not nullify that ownership. The releaser destroyed the property.
The owner could keep the game by confining it.
3
State the Sahibayn's counter-argument
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: The releaser is not liable because he is commanding good and forbidding evil (amr bil-ma'ruf nahy an al-munkar). The Muhrim should release the game anyway.
They see the release as a virtuous act, not a transgression.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa responds: The Muhrim is not required to release game he owned before ihram (as established in a prior ruling). Thus the releaser is not commanding good but destroying property. This distinguishes from the case where game is taken in ihram (no ownership).
The earlier ruling: a Muhrim may keep game owned before ihram.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's (consistent with ownership rights). Practical consequence: If the game is released and taken by a non-Muhrim, the releaser may owe compensation in Abu Hanifa's view, but not in the Sahibayn's view.
Thamarah al-khilaf: liability vs. no liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أرسل صيداً في يد محرم إن أخذه حلالاً ضَمِن، وإلا فلا. فإن قتل محرم صيد مثله، فكل يجزئ جزاء كاملاً، ورَجَعَ آخِذُهُ على قاتله. وما به دم [1] قوله: ومن أرسل ... الخ؛ قال في «الهداية»: فإن أصاب حلال صيداً ثم أحرم فأرسله من يده غيره، يضمن عند أبي حنيفة له، وقالا: لا يضمن؛ لأنَّ المرسل آمر بالمعروف ناه عن المنكر. وله: أنه ملك الصيد بالأخذ ملكاً مُحترماً، فلا يبطل احترامه بإحرامه، وقد أتلفه المرسل فيضمنه، بخلاف ما إذا أخذه في حالة الإحرام؛ لأنه لم يملكه، والواجب عليه ترك التعرّض، ويمكنه ذلك بأن يخلّيه في بيت، فإذا قطع يده كان متعدياً فيضمنه. [٢] قوله: أخذه؛ أي أخذ المحرم الآخر ذلك الصيد حال كونه غير محرم. [٣] قوله: فكل يجزئ؛ أي كلّ من المحرم الآخذ والمحـرم القاتل يؤدّي جـزاء الصيد، أما الآخذ فبأخذه، وأما القاتل فبقتله ورجع الآخذ على القاتل بما وجب عليه أداؤه: أي ضمنه ما أداه؛ لأنّ أخذه وإن كان موجباً للضمان، لكنه كان على شرف السقوط، فإنّه كان يمكنه أن يخلّيه، وإنّما تقرر عليه الجزاء بقتل القاتل. [٤] قوله: ومـا بـه ... الخ؛ يعني كل فعل يجب به دم واحد على المفرد بالحج، فعلى القارن به إن فعله دمان، والمراد به فعل ما هو من محظورات الإحرام: أي ما حَرُمَ بسبب نفس الإحرام إلا من حيث كونه حجّاً أو عمرة، ولا ما حرم عليه بسبب غير الإحرام، وذلك كاللبس والتطيب، فخرج ما لو ترك واجباً كالسعي والرمي، فإنّ فيه كفارة واحدة على القارن أيضاً، وخرج أيضاً ما لو قطع حشيش الحرم، فلا يتعدد الجزاء به، وزيادة التفصيل في هذا البحث في «البحر الرائق».
And whoever releases game from the hand of a Muhrim, if a non-Muhrim takes it, he is liable; otherwise not. If a Muhrim kills game of his like, then each pays a full compensation, and the taker has recourse against the killer. And whatever requires one blood penalty. [1] His saying 'and whoever releases...' etc.; it is said in al-Hidaya: 'If a non-Muhrim captures game then enters ihram, then someone else releases it from his hand, according to Abu Hanifa he is liable, but the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: he is not liable, because the releaser is commanding good and forbidding evil. And for him (Abu Hanifa): the person owned the game by taking it as a respected ownership, so its respect is not nullified by his ihram, and the releaser has destroyed it, so he is liable, unlike if he took it in the state of ihram, because he did not own it, and his duty is to avoid harming it, and he can do that by confining it in a house; if someone cuts his hand, he is transgressor and is liable.' [2] His saying 'his taking'; i.e., the other Muhrim takes that game while he is non-Muhrim. [3] His saying 'so each pays full compensation'; i.e., each of the Muhrim who took and the Muhrim who killed pays compensation for the game; as for the taker, for his taking, and as for the killer, for his killing; and the taker has recourse against the killer for what he had to pay: i.e., he indemnifies him for what he paid; because his taking, although it necessitated liability, was on the verge of falling, for he could have released it, and the compensation was only settled upon him due to the killer's killing. [4] His saying 'and whatever requires one blood penalty'; meaning every action that requires one blood penalty (dam) for a Mufrid performing Hajj alone, for a Qarin performing Hajj and Umrah together, if he does it, he owes two blood penalties. What is meant is actions that are prohibitions of ihram due to ihram itself in terms of Hajj or Umrah, not that which is prohibited due to other reasons, such as wearing sewn clothes and applying perfume. This excludes leaving an obligatory act like Sa'i and stoning, for which there is only one expiation on the Qarin as well; also excludes cutting the grass of the Haram, for which the penalty is not multiplied. Further detail on this topic is in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This passage contains several distinct rulings from the Hajj chapter on offenses (jinaayat). The first ruling addresses a dispute within the Hanafi school: if a non-Muhrim captures game, then enters ihram, and someone else releases the game from his hand, is the releaser liable? According to Imam Abu Hanifa, he is liable because the owner had valid ownership of the game, and ihram does not invalidate that ownership. The releaser has effectively destroyed the property, so he must compensate. However, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that the releaser is not liable because he is simply commanding good and forbidding evil (since the Muhrim should release the game anyway). Abu Hanifa's position is that the Muhrim is not required to release game he owned before ihram, so the releaser is a transgressor. The second ruling: if one Muhrim takes (i.e., captures) game and another Muhrim kills it, both must pay a full compensation (jaza'). The taker pays for taking, the killer for killing. However, the taker can then recover from the killer what he paid, because his liability was contingent and was only finalized by the killing. The third ruling: for a person performing Hajj alone (Mufrid), certain violations require one blood penalty (dam); for a person performing Hajj and Umrah together (Qarin), the same violation requires two blood penalties (one for Hajj, one for Umrah). This applies only to prohibitions specific to ihram (like wearing perfume, hunting, etc.), not to general prohibitions or to leaving obligatory acts (which incur only one penalty even for a Qarin).

Key Terms
جزاء (jaza'): Compensation or penalty paid for violating ihram prohibitions, such as killing or taking game.
ملك محترم (milk muhtaram): Respected ownership; property that is legally inviolable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is a person who releases game from a Muhrim's hand liable?
A) Because the Muhrim was about to commit a violation.
B) Because the releaser destroyed the Muhrim's property that was lawfully owned.
C) Because the game was haram for the Muhrim to keep.
D) Because the releaser did not have permission.

Key Takeaway
For game owned before ihram, releasing it is a transgression (destruction of property), not commanding good, so the releaser is liable under Abu Hanifa's relied-upon view.

Multiple Muhrim Offenders: Taker and Killer Both Liable
Ruling on multiple Muhrims involved with the same game.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when one Muhrim takes (captures) game and another Muhrim kills it, including the recourse right of the taker against the killer.

Core Idea
Both pay full jaza', but the taker recovers from the killer because his liability was contingent.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: two Muhrims are involved with the same game — one captures it, the other kills it. Who owes compensation? The text gives a clear ruling: each pays a full compensation (jaza'), but the taker has the right to recover from the killer what he paid. Why? Because the taker's liability was on the verge of falling (he could have released the game) and was only finalized by the killing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and actions
Two Muhrims: one takes (captures) game, the other kills the same game. Both are in ihram.
The game is of a type that is lawful for a non-Muhrim.
2
State the ruling for the taker
The taker must pay a full jaza' (compensation) for his act of taking. Taking game in ihram is a violation requiring compensation.
The taker's act is independent of the killing.
3
State the ruling for the killer
The killer also pays a full jaza' for killing.
Killing game in ihram is also a violation.
4
Explain the recourse (ruju')
The taker can then recover from the killer the amount he paid. Reason: the taker's liability was contingent — he could have released the game and avoided payment. The killing finalized his liability, so the killer is ultimately responsible.
The taker's obligation became settled only because of the killer's act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

D) Neither pays compensation because the game was taken.

Key Takeaway
When two Muhrims both commit violations on the same game (taking and killing), each pays full jaza', but the taker can recover from the killer because the killer's act made the taker's liability definite.

Hajj — Pilgrimage
intermediate
7 min
One Dam for Mufrid, Two for Qarin: Multiplicity of Penalties
Ruling on multiplicity of dam for Mufrid vs. Qarin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a Qarin must offer two dam for a single ihram prohibition violation (except leaving obligations or cutting Haram grass), while a Mufrid offers one.

Core Idea
A Qarin faces double penalties for ihram prohibitions because he is under two states of ihram (Hajj and Umrah).

This lesson explains a key distinction in ihram penalties: a person performing Hajj alone (Mufrid) who commits a prohibition (like wearing perfume or hunting) must offer one blood penalty (dam). But a person performing Hajj and Umrah together (Qarin) must offer two dam for the same act. Why? Because the Qarin is in two states of ihram simultaneously. However, this doubling does not apply to all violations—it excludes leaving obligatory acts (like sa'i) and cutting the grass of the Haram.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
For a Mufrid (one performing only Hajj), a single prohibition (e.g., wearing perfume, hunting) requires one dam. For a Qarin (performing Hajj and Umrah together), the same act requires two dam.
The reason: the Qarin has two ihrams.
2
Understand the scope: which violations are included?
The doubling applies only to prohibitions that are specific to ihram itself (muhazirat al-ihram) such as wearing sewn clothes, applying perfume, covering the head, etc. It does not apply to violations due to other reasons (e.g., leaving an obligatory act like sa'i or ramy, which carry only one penalty even for a Qarin).
Also excluded: cutting the grass of the Haram (penalty does not multiply).
3
Apply the exception: leaving obligations
If a Qarin leaves a wajib (obligatory act) of Hajj or Umrah, such as sa'i or stoning, he owes only one dam, not two. Because the wajib is a single requirement, and the doubling is only for prohibitions.
The text explicitly excludes this.
4
Apply the exception: cutting Haram grass
If a Qarin cuts grass from the Haram (sacred precinct), the penalty does not multiply. The reason is that this prohibition is tied to the land (Haram), not to the state of ihram.
Thus only one dam or its equivalent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أرسل صيداً في يد محرم إن أخذه حلالاً ضَمِن، وإلا فلا. فإن قتل محرم صيد مثله، فكل يجزئ جزاء كاملاً، ورَجَعَ آخِذُهُ على قاتله. وما به دم [1] قوله: ومن أرسل ... الخ؛ قال في «الهداية»: فإن أصاب حلال صيداً ثم أحرم فأرسله من يده غيره، يضمن عند أبي حنيفة له، وقالا: لا يضمن؛ لأنَّ المرسل آمر بالمعروف ناه عن المنكر. وله: أنه ملك الصيد بالأخذ ملكاً مُحترماً، فلا يبطل احترامه بإحرامه، وقد أتلفه المرسل فيضمنه، بخلاف ما إذا أخذه في حالة الإحرام؛ لأنه لم يملكه، والواجب عليه ترك التعرّض، ويمكنه ذلك بأن يخلّيه في بيت، فإذا قطع يده كان متعدياً فيضمنه. [٢] قوله: أخذه؛ أي أخذ المحرم الآخر ذلك الصيد حال كونه غير محرم. [٣] قوله: فكل يجزئ؛ أي كلّ من المحرم الآخذ والمحـرم القاتل يؤدّي جـزاء الصيد، أما الآخذ فبأخذه، وأما القاتل فبقتله ورجع الآخذ على القاتل بما وجب عليه أداؤه: أي ضمنه ما أداه؛ لأنّ أخذه وإن كان موجباً للضمان، لكنه كان على شرف السقوط، فإنّه كان يمكنه أن يخلّيه، وإنّما تقرر عليه الجزاء بقتل القاتل. [٤] قوله: ومـا بـه ... الخ؛ يعني كل فعل يجب به دم واحد على المفرد بالحج، فعلى القارن به إن فعله دمان، والمراد به فعل ما هو من محظورات الإحرام: أي ما حَرُمَ بسبب نفس الإحرام إلا من حيث كونه حجّاً أو عمرة، ولا ما حرم عليه بسبب غير الإحرام، وذلك كاللبس والتطيب، فخرج ما لو ترك واجباً كالسعي والرمي، فإنّ فيه كفارة واحدة على القارن أيضاً، وخرج أيضاً ما لو قطع حشيش الحرم، فلا يتعدد الجزاء به، وزيادة التفصيل في هذا البحث في «البحر الرائق».
And whoever releases game from the hand of a Muhrim, if a non-Muhrim takes it, he is liable; otherwise not. If a Muhrim kills game of his like, then each pays a full compensation, and the taker has recourse against the killer. And whatever requires one blood penalty. [1] His saying 'and whoever releases...' etc.; it is said in al-Hidaya: 'If a non-Muhrim captures game then enters ihram, then someone else releases it from his hand, according to Abu Hanifa he is liable, but the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: he is not liable, because the releaser is commanding good and forbidding evil. And for him (Abu Hanifa): the person owned the game by taking it as a respected ownership, so its respect is not nullified by his ihram, and the releaser has destroyed it, so he is liable, unlike if he took it in the state of ihram, because he did not own it, and his duty is to avoid harming it, and he can do that by confining it in a house; if someone cuts his hand, he is transgressor and is liable.' [2] His saying 'his taking'; i.e., the other Muhrim takes that game while he is non-Muhrim. [3] His saying 'so each pays full compensation'; i.e., each of the Muhrim who took and the Muhrim who killed pays compensation for the game; as for the taker, for his taking, and as for the killer, for his killing; and the taker has recourse against the killer for what he had to pay: i.e., he indemnifies him for what he paid; because his taking, although it necessitated liability, was on the verge of falling, for he could have released it, and the compensation was only settled upon him due to the killer's killing. [4] His saying 'and whatever requires one blood penalty'; meaning every action that requires one blood penalty (dam) for a Mufrid performing Hajj alone, for a Qarin performing Hajj and Umrah together, if he does it, he owes two blood penalties. What is meant is actions that are prohibitions of ihram due to ihram itself in terms of Hajj or Umrah, not that which is prohibited due to other reasons, such as wearing sewn clothes and applying perfume. This excludes leaving an obligatory act like Sa'i and stoning, for which there is only one expiation on the Qarin as well; also excludes cutting the grass of the Haram, for which the penalty is not multiplied. Further detail on this topic is in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This passage contains several distinct rulings from the Hajj chapter on offenses (jinaayat). The first ruling addresses a dispute within the Hanafi school: if a non-Muhrim captures game, then enters ihram, and someone else releases the game from his hand, is the releaser liable? According to Imam Abu Hanifa, he is liable because the owner had valid ownership of the game, and ihram does not invalidate that ownership. The releaser has effectively destroyed the property, so he must compensate. However, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that the releaser is not liable because he is simply commanding good and forbidding evil (since the Muhrim should release the game anyway). Abu Hanifa's position is that the Muhrim is not required to release game he owned before ihram, so the releaser is a transgressor. The second ruling: if one Muhrim takes (i.e., captures) game and another Muhrim kills it, both must pay a full compensation (jaza'). The taker pays for taking, the killer for killing. However, the taker can then recover from the killer what he paid, because his liability was contingent and was only finalized by the killing. The third ruling: for a person performing Hajj alone (Mufrid), certain violations require one blood penalty (dam); for a person performing Hajj and Umrah together (Qarin), the same violation requires two blood penalties (one for Hajj, one for Umrah). This applies only to prohibitions specific to ihram (like wearing perfume, hunting, etc.), not to general prohibitions or to leaving obligatory acts (which incur only one penalty even for a Qarin).

Key Terms
دم (dam): A blood penalty, usually a sacrifice of a sheep or goat, for certain ihram violations.
قارن (qarin): A person performing Hajj and Umrah together in one state of ihram.
مفرد (mufrid): A person performing only Hajj, without Umrah in the same ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following actions would require a Qarin to offer two dam?
A) Leaving the sa'i (walking between Safa and Marwah).
B) Cutting grass from the Haram.
C) Applying perfume while in ihram.
D) Missing the stoning ritual.

Key Takeaway
A Qarin must offer two dam for a single ihram prohibition (like perfume or hunting), but only one dam for leaving an obligatory act or cutting Haram grass.
